[3] Due to my participation in the various events of this story, for those who are fascinated by Mr. Philo Vance's fame, I can't arouse any interest in them. Therefore, in order to satisfy my vanity as a woman, I will make a brief and concise introduction of myself.
[4] I am very young, so young that even the most strict standards cannot refute it. I was born with a pair of sparkling blue eyes - how many imaginative gentlemen have described them so - shining like stars at night, as blue as the sky. A young student from Heidelberg University Preparatory School once compared my hair to honey, but an American lady I met in the French Riviera resort of Antibes was sarcastic and said they looked like a bunch of rotten straw. Recently, when I stood next to the most favored model number 16 at the Claris Salon in Paris, I found that my figure was almost identical to that of the proud and exquisite woman with the charm and perfect beauty.
[5] I am physically intact, with no physical defects, and - a point that even Mr. Vance himself would agree - I have a flexible and clear mind. Some have said that one of my main charms is "innocence and frankness without modesty", and this, I believe, will be proven false in the following content.
[6] That's about it. However, I do feel that I can be described as a "drifting Nordic". From the time I started wearing pigtails and wearing a sailor suit as a schoolgirl, I have been wandering. My journey has made brief stops at some random resting places: for example, I spent two years at a terrible bridal school in London; I spent fourteen months in the artist's haven of the Left Bank of the Seine in Paris until I gave up and realized that my "Percy Sam" name would never be comparable to Gauguin, Matisse, and so on.
[7] I have visited the East like Marco Polo; I have also stormed the gates of Rome like the ancient Carthaginian military commander Hannibal. Furthermore, I am scientifically minded: I tasted absinthe in Tunisia, North Africa, drank the local wine in Lyon, France, and enjoyed the flavor of brandy in Lisbon, Portugal; I also climbed up to the Acropolis on the top of Mount Lycabettus, Athens, and drank the intoxicating air of the poetic sea with a longing delight.
[8] All of this, of course, is thanks to my affluent family, and I have always had a unique character by my side - an old female companion with astigmatism and a good sense of humor.
[9] Travel is like whipped cream, the more you eat, the more you crave, but too much of it can make you tired. At this time, the traveler is like an old man, wanting to return to nature and eat some simple home-cooked food. So, with the determination of a young girl, I bid farewell to the charming old companion in Algiers, North Africa, and set sail to return home. My father's roast beef dinner welcomed me back, and my stomach was comfortable. To be honest, when I planned to bring a well-worn but still charming French edition of Madame Chatterley's Lover into New York, he was really scared. In the two years at the bridal school, this novel had accompanied me through many Nazi-inspired nights in my room; but after solving this little problem, he pushed me out of customs and then we, these two unfamiliar wolves, silently returned to our downtown apartment.
[10] Now, after reading "X's Tragedy" and "Y's Tragedy", I have found that my great and robust father, Inspector Sam, has not mentioned his wandering daughter even once in those passionate chapters. In the kisses at the dock, I realized from his surprised and loving gaze that this was not out of callousness, but that we had drifted
[11] Here follows the story of Mr. Leigh and the case of Allen Darrow, the convict at Algonquin Prison. All of this is just a necessary prelude to explain how the eccentric Percy Sam became involved in this murder case.
[12] During my journey home, I suddenly realized that what I most longed for was to meet this strange genius who lived in the magical castle by the Hudson River.
[13] However, I found that my father was immersed in his work and had no interest in anything else. After retiring from the New York Police Department, he naturally felt bored and aimless after a lifetime of crime cases; he inevitably plunged back into private detective work, and due to his reputation, his venture was a great success from the start.
[14] As for me, I had nothing to do and felt that the education and lifestyle I had received abroad were incompatible with serious life. Perhaps it was inevitable that I resumed everything that had been interrupted in the past. I spent a lot of time in my father's office, pestering him with his complaints and grievances as I used to. He seemed to consider his daughter as a decorative accessory, but my stubbornness finally softened him. On several occasions, he even let me conduct some simple investigations, from which I learned some terms and the modern psychology of crime - these rough training was very helpful for my later analysis of the Darrow case.
[15] But there was another more helpful thing that happened, both to my father and me, that we were surprised to find that I had an extraordinary intuition in observation and reasoning. This also made me realize that I had a very special gift, perhaps stemming from my early environment and my never-ending interest in crime.
[16] My father once complained, "Petty (nickname for Pencils), having you this damn girl around makes me feel embarrassed. God, it's just like being with Philo Vance again!"
[17] I replied, "Dear Inspector, that compliment is greatly appreciated. When do you plan to introduce me to him?"
[18] Three months after my return, the opportunity came unexpectedly. It began as a very simple incident, but it later - just like many clichéd plots - developed into a series of amazing developments, even scaring me, a girl who loves criminal investigation.
[19] One day, a tall, well-dressed man with gray hair came to my father's office. His anxious expression showed that he wanted my father's help. His business card had the golden imprint of "Elias Clay", and he looked at me sharply with his sharp eyes. He sat down, tightly gripping his cane, and introduced himself in a simple and severe manner, like a French banker.
[20] He was the owner of Clay Marble Industries, with the main quarry located in Tirendine County, New York, and his office and residence in Rye, New York. The matter he came to ask my father to investigate was very sensitive and confidential, which was why he traveled all the way to the countryside to find a detective. He insisted that we be extremely, extremely careful...
[21] "I see," Father said with a smile. "Would you like a cigar? Did your safe's money get stolen?"
[22] "No, really! I have... oh... a mysterious partner."
[23] "Ha," Father said, "Tell me about it."
[24] This anonymous partner - since he's now out in the open, there's no reason to call him anonymous - is Dr. Ella Fritch, whose brother is Senator Joel Fritch of Tirellin County, a well-known figure. From my father's furrowed brow, I could tell that Senator Fritch was not a very honest man. Mr. Clay modestly referred to himself as "an old-fashioned honest businessman" and now seemed regretful for letting Dr. Fritch join the company. I deduced that Dr. Fritch must not be a good person, and Mr. Clay suspected that some of the business contracts he handled were not legitimate. The company's business was good - too good to be true. A stack of contracts from various states found their way to Clay Marble Company. Therefore, it was necessary to conduct a discreet and thorough investigation in this situation.
[25] "No evidence?" Father asked.
[26] "Not a shred, sir. He's too slick for that. I only have suspicions. Can you take on this case?" Mr. Clay placed three large checks on the table.
[27] The father looked at me. "Should we, Petty?"
[28] I hesitated. "We're quite busy. We'll have to put other things on hold if we take this on."
[29] Mr. Clay stared at me for a moment before speaking. "I have a suggestion, sir. I don't want Dr. Fawcett to suspect you, but I need your help. How about if Miss Sam and you come to my house as guests? Miss Sam's presence might – allow me to be frank – make things easier."
[30] "Dad, we can handle it," I said, and began making arrangements.
[31] Elias Clay returned to northern New York that day. The next two days, we took care of some pending work, and by Sunday evening, we had packed our bags and were ready to go to Rye.
[32] I remember, when the telegram arrived, I was sitting with my legs stretched out in front of the fireplace, sipping a fine brandy – which I had smuggled in and managed to fool the young and friendly customs officer.
[33] Father slapped his leg and said, "Bruno is still the same! Well, Petty, your chance has come. You've been nagging me about that thing, and now you can do it."
[34] He threw the telegram at me, which read:
[35] Your old comrade is planning to fly out tomorrow to wish Master Rain a happy 70th birthday. He wants to give him a surprise. I know Master Rain has been ill recently and needs someone to cheer him up. If a busy governor can make time, you can do it too.
[36] Looking forward to meeting you there.
[37] "Oh, great!" I exclaimed, spilling half of my drink on my pajamas, "Do you think, uh - do you think he'll like me?"
[38] "Philip Rayne, the man," my father murmured, "is not... not... he hates women. But it seems I have to take you with me. You should go to bed," he chuckled, "Well, Petty, dream well for tomorrow, we have to make that old man surprised. And, uh - Petty, do you really have to drink? I'm not one of those old-fashioned fathers, but -"
[39] I pecked his ugly beak-shaped nose. Poor old dad, he had already done his best.
[40] The Hamlet Manor, where Mr. Philip Rayne resides, is situated on the hills by the Hudson River, and the scenery along the way is just as my father had described, even more enchanting than my imagination. I have traveled through many ancient European landscapes, but I have never seen a place as breathtaking as this. Dense forests, clear roads, a few clouds floating in the sky, a tranquil blue river winding through the foot, and the beauty and serenity are even more beautiful than the Rhine. And that castle is probably transported from the ancient peaks of England by magic carpet, magnificent, grandiose, and ancient.
[41] We crossed a quaint wooden bridge, through a private forest that resembled Robin Hood's Sherwood Forest — I couldn't help but wish that Robin Hood's lively companion Friar Tuck would suddenly jump out from behind and scare us — and then through the castle gates, into the courtyard of the estate. Everywhere we looked were smiling faces, most of them quite old, and Mr. Philip Leroy had taken in many elderly, ailing artists in the castle. My father told me that Mr. Leroy's generosity had sheltered many people.
[42] We met the state governor, Bruno, who was waiting for us. His expression was cheerful, with a square face, short stature, a high forehead, bright and wise eyes, a protruding chin, and a determined look. A state trooper followed him as a bodyguard, constantly patrolling nearby.
[43] But I was too excited to pay much attention to the governor. An old man was walking towards us through the hedges of privet and yew — he looked so old, I couldn't help but be surprised. I had always thought that Mr. Philip Leroy was in his prime, a tall and energetic man, but now I realized that time had been so unkind to him. His broad shoulders had become hunched, his once thick head of hair was now sparse, and wrinkles had etched themselves onto his face and hands, slowing his once lively steps. However, his eyes remained youthful — steady, clear, wise, witty, and talented. His cheeks were rosy, and at first, it seemed he didn't notice me, only gripping my father and Governor Bruno's hands and murmuring, "Oh, it's so good to have you here, so good."
[44] I'm usually not one to get emotional, but at that moment, I felt a lump in my throat and tears welling up in my eyes…
[45] My father rubbed his nose and spoke in a hoarse voice: "Mr. Philip Leroy, allow me to introduce, this is — my daughter."
[46] His old hand grasped mine and looked into my eyes solemnly, "Dear, welcome to Hamlet Manor."
[47] Then I said something that I would be ashamed of later, to be honest, I wanted to show off my intelligence and wit. I had been looking forward to this meeting for a long time, and under the influence of my subconscious, I had completely changed in this moment.
[48] Anyway, I blurted out, "It's an honor, Mr. Renard, you don't know how much – I really –" and then winked – I'm sure it was a wink – and then, without thinking, said, "I think you're planning to write a memoir!"
[49] Of course, I immediately regretted saying such a thoughtless and ignorant thing. I bit my lips and felt extremely embarrassed. My father gasped, and Bruno the Governor was completely stunned. As for Mr. Renard, he raised his eyebrows, staring at my face for a long time with a sharp gaze, and then rubbed his hands and chuckled, "Child, this is quite amazing. Sheriff, you've kept this girl hidden for so long, I won't let you off. What's your name?"
[50] "Percy," I whispered.
[51] "Ah, a Puritan tone. Chief, I dare say that this name is yours, not that of your wife.” He chuckled again, suddenly taking my arm, "You two old fossils come along, we'll catch up later. Amazing, truly amazing!"
[52] "Well," Mr. Lane cleared his throat, waiting for us to come to our senses, "now, Percy, let's look at those amazing things you said.” His voice was in my ear, with a special timbre, deep, calm, and full, like a fine French wine, Morgon, "You said I was considering writing a memoir, is that right? Indeed, what else did you see with those pretty eyes, dear?"
[53] "Oh, I'm really sorry for saying those things... I mean, I shouldn't... I don't want to take up your time, Mr. Lane, you and Governor Bruno and my father haven't seen each other for a long time."
[54] "Nonsense, child. I'm sure we old men still have a lot to learn from Percy." He chuckled again, "Another symbol of aging. What else did you see, Percy?"
[55] "Uh,” I breathed a sigh of relief, "you're learning to type, Mr. Lane."
[56] "Ah!” He looked startled. Father stared at me as if he didn't recognize me.
[57] "And,” I continued modestly, "You're self-taught in typing, Mr. Raine. You're using the touch typing method, not the random key pressing method for beginners.”
[58] "Good heavens! It's karma.” He turned to Father, smiling, "Sergeant, you've truly given birth to a genius. Or maybe you've told Percy some tales about me.”
[59] "Damn! I'm just as surprised as you. What else can I tell her? I don't even know these things. Is she telling the truth?”
[60] Governor Bruno stroked his chin: "Miss Sam, I think the state government in Albany could hire you…"
[61] "Hey, don't get off track," Jury Rine muttered, his eyes shining. "It's a challenge. It's reasoning, eh? Since Peacham could guess it, there must be a clue, I see... From the moment we met, right? First, I walked through the bushes. Then I greeted the inspector, and you, Bruno. Then Peacham and I met, and—handshake. Eureka! Remarkable reasoning... Ha! The hand, of course!” He quickly examined his hand and nodded with a smile. "Dear, it's amazing. Oh, yes! Of course! Learned typing, eh? Inspector, what did you see on my palm?"
[62] He spread his hand, with veins showing, in front of his father's nose. His father blinked.
[63] "Tell me, what clues did you see? It's all right there on my hand!"
[64] We laughed.
[65] "Inspector, this again proves my belief in the importance of paying attention to details in the investigative process. Details are that each of my four fingertips on both hands is worn and cracked, but my thumbs are intact and well-manicured. Clearly, the only activity that could damage all the nails without harming the thumb is typing—learning to type, because the nails are not accustomed to the impact of fingertips on the keys, and they haven't healed yet... Brilliant, Peacham!"
[66] "Well..." the father seemed a bit unhappy.
[67] "Oh, don't be like that, Officer." Mr. Rine laughed, "You're always a skeptic. Yes, yes, Percy, too clever! As for the typing method, it's a clever deduction. Because most beginners use the so-called feel method, which only uses two fingers, so only two nails will be worn; on the contrary, the typing method must use all the fingers except the thumb. He closed his eyes, "So I must have planned to write a memoir! Darling, making bold conclusions based on observed phenomena proves that you have the gift of intuition, observation, and reasoning. Bruno, do you know how this young lady came to her conclusion?"
[68] "Not at all." The governor admitted.
[69] "It's a damn trick," the father muttered, but I noticed that his cigar was out and his hand was trembling slightly.
[70] Mr. Rine chuckled again: "It's simple! Percy would think, why did a seventy-year-old old man suddenly decide to learn typing? It's too abnormal, because he never intended to learn it for fifty years! Right, Percy?"
[71] "Exactly, Mr. Rine, you seem to understand quickly..."
[72] "So, you think, the only possible reason for someone of this age to do such a thing is that he knows his good days are numbered and wants to write down some personal long memories before the end of his life. Of course! It's amazing. But I don't understand, Percy, how did you know I learned on my own? That's a good guess, but my life has always been..."
[73] "Well," I whispered, "it's just a little trick. The basis of reasoning is that, generally speaking, if someone teaches you, they would use all the methods to teach beginners, and they would take the touch typing method. But to help the students remember the position of each letter and not look at the keyboard, the teacher would stick a rubber pad on the keyboard to cover the letters. But if your keyboard is covered with a rubber pad, Mr. Rine, your nails wouldn't break! Therefore, you must have learned on your own."
[74] "Damn it," the father said, staring at me as if I were some sort of bird or freakish creature. However, my little performance of showing off my intelligence did please Mr. Rine, who immediately treated me as a colleague or someone of a higher rank. However, it seems that the father was a bit upset, as he and Mr. Rine have always been at odds in terms of their investigative methods.
[75] We spent the entire afternoon quietly walking in the garden, visiting Mr. Rine's village of pebbles for his colleagues, drinking black beer at the Mermaid Hotel, visiting his private theater, and the vast library that contained an impressive and unique collection of books about Shakespeare. It was the most exciting afternoon of my life, but unfortunately, good times always pass too quickly.
[76] The lavish dinner was held in a medieval-style banquet hall, filled with guests from all over who had come to Hamlet Manor to celebrate Mr. Reen's birthday. They chattered and laughed loudly as they ate and drank. After dinner, the four of us retired to Mr. Reen's private living room, where we sipped Turkish coffee and liqueurs. An old, hunched-over man kept coming and going in the room, looking very old and old. Mr. Reen confirmed that he was over a hundred years old. This was the remarkable Quincey, whom Mr. Reen nicknamed Caliban, and I had heard of and read about in many outstanding novels.
[77] The flickering fire in the fireplace and the tranquility of the oak walls made me feel liberated from the noise of the banquet. I was tired and gratefully relaxed, leaning back in the solemn Tudor-style armchair. My father had gray hair and broad shoulders; the state governor had a protruding chin and was full of vigor; Mr. Reen's face was noble…
[78] It's nice to be here.
[79] Mr. Reen was in high spirits, constantly asking the governor and my father various questions, but when it came to his own affairs, he refused to reveal any details.
[80] "I've had terrible days," he whispered softly, "like withered leaves falling. As Shakespeare said, I should take care of my old body. My doctor is trying to keep my body from being deformed, I'm getting old." Then he smiled softly and waved his hand, "Don't talk about this old man. Detective, you just said you and Percy were planning to go to the interior?"
[81] "Mr. Petti and I are going to the north to handle a case."
[82] "Ah," Mr. Rene's nostrils flared, "handling a case, I almost want to go with you. What kind of case is it?"
[83] The father shrugged. "We don't know much. It's not the kind you'd be interested in. But Governor, you probably would be. I think your brother from Tirelund, Joel Fawcett, is involved in this case."
[84] "It's ridiculous," the governor reacted strongly. "Joel Fawcett is not my friend, and I would be angry if he said he was like me. He's a bad guy who organized a violent gang in Tirelund."
[85] "Good news," the father smiled. "It looks like we'll be very busy. Do you know anything about his brother, Dr. Ella Fawcett?"
[86] I could see that Governor Bruno was surprised. His eyes lit up as he stared into the fire: "Senator Fawcett is the worst kind of con artist politician, but his brother Ella is the real boss behind the scenes. On the surface, it doesn't look like it, but I dare say he's the black hand behind his brother."
[87] "That's right," Father frowned, "Dr. Fawcett is an anonymous partner of Mr. Clay, a marble entrepreneur in Riz City. He believes that some of the Fawcett Agency's contracts are questionable and wants me to investigate. It seems quite ordinary, but finding evidence is difficult."
[88] "I won't envy you, Dr. Fawcett is a slippery old fox. Clay, I know him, he seems nice and has no problems... I'll be particularly interested because the Fawcett brothers have a tough fight coming up this fall."
[89] Mr. Reen closed his eyes and sat weakly in his chair, smiling feebly. I suddenly realized that he couldn't hear anything. Father often mentioned his deafness and lip-reading. But now, his eyelids had shut out the world.
[90] I shook my head impatiently, shaking off those irrelevant thoughts and concentrating on the conversation in front of me. The governor described the situation in Riz City and Tireldon County in his usual exaggerated tone. Next month, a fierce election battle is expected to take place, with a young and energetic district attorney - John Hume - already gaining the support of the opposition party and nominated to run for senator. He is well-liked and admired by local voters for his reputation as a clean and honest prosecutor, which poses a serious threat to Fawcett's re-election. With the support of one of the state's most cunning politicians, Rufus Cotton, young John Hume is campaigning for reform - I think, considering Senator Fawcett's past notorious behavior, the call for reform hits the mark - "the most greedy money-grabbing politician in New York State," Governor Bruno described Senator Fawcett, and there is a state prison in Riz City, Acoma Prison.
[91] Mr. Reen opens his eyes and looks intently at Governor Bruno's lips for a while, and I don't understand why he is so enthusiastic. When he mentions the prison, I see his old eyes suddenly light up.
[92] "Acoma, huh?" he calls, "It's so interesting. A few years ago - Governor Bruno, you weren't governor yet - Vice Governor Morton and Warden Magnus arranged for me to visit the prison, a strange place. I met an old friend there - the chaplain of the prison, Father Mueller. I've known him for a long time - I think, before I met you. He was the guardian of the Black Street district in Manhattan, New York City. Chief, if you see Father Mueller, please convey my sincere regards. It's a great opportunity. My days of investigating prisons are in the past... Are you leaving, Governor Bruno?"
[93] Governor Bruno reluctantly gets up: "I have to go. There are important matters in the legislature, and I've managed to sneak away in the midst of all this."
[94] Mr. Reen's smile disappears, and the lines of age return to his weathered face: "Oh, don't be like that, Governor Bruno. You can't just leave us. Why - we've just started talking..."
[95] "I'm sorry, old man, I really have to go. Sam, will you stay?"
[96] The father stroked his chin, and Mr. Reen quickly interjected: "Chief and Percy, of course, will stay overnight. They're not in a hurry."
[97] "Well, I think we can postpone this Fossett thing," the father said as he stretched his legs and took a deep breath, and I nodded.
[98] However, if we had gone to Richmond that evening, the course of events might have been completely different. At least, we could have seen Dr. Fossett before he embarked on his mysterious journey, and then we could have unraveled many of the subsequent mysteries... However, at the time, we were completely under the spell of Hamlet Manor and stayed overnight.
[99] Governor Bruno left, surrounded by a group of state troopers, with a sense of regret. After he left, I soon fell asleep in the soft sheets of the Tudor-style bed, feeling happy and exhausted, completely unaware of what lay ahead.
[100] Chapter 2: Meeting the Dead
[101] Leeds is a charming and bustling small town nestled at the foot of a conical hill, the center of the agricultural county, surrounded by rolling fields and blue hills. The imposing high walls topped with sentry boxes and the ugly chimneys of the mill prison loomed over the serene countryside and town. Even the green forest on the hill could not soften the scene. I was curious. How many desperate men had been sentenced to this desolate wall, longing for the cool forest just beyond the prison walls, but as far away as Mars to them?
[102] "You'll understand, Petty," my father told me as we stepped off the train and into a taxi, "Most of the people there are hardened criminals. Child, don't waste too much sympathy on them."
[103] Perhaps a lifetime of dealing with criminals had made him callous, but to me, it did not mean they should be cut off from the lush fields and clear skies, and I did not believe any sin could be so grave as to warrant such a cruel punishment.
[104] We were both silent during the short journey to the home of Elijah Clay.
[105] Clay's white-columned colonial-style mansion sat on a hillside on the outskirts of town, and Elijah Clay himself was waiting for us on the porch. He was a gracious and attentive host, and his demeanor gave no hint that we were hired hands. He had the butler show us to comfortable bedrooms, and we immediately felt at ease. For the rest of the afternoon, he regaled us with stories about Leeds and himself - as if we were old friends. We learned that he was a widower, and he spoke mournfully of his late wife, one of her greatest regrets being that she had no daughter to take her place. I found myself greatly changing my opinion of Elijah Clay: when he had come to New York to find us, I had thought him a crass businessman. Over the next few calm days, I grew to like him more and more.
[106] My father and Mr. Clay spent several hours in the study, followed by a whole day at the quarry, located on the banks of the Chard River, several miles from Leeds. My father was gathering information about the case, and from his constant grumbling since the first day, it was clear that he expected the case to be difficult, time-consuming, and ultimately fruitless.
[107] "There's not a shred of written evidence, Petty," he muttered to me, complaining, "This Mr. Fosdick is a demon in disguise, no wonder Mr. Clay came to us for help. This case is much more difficult than I thought."
[108] Despite my sympathy for him, investigating this case won't help much.
[109] Dr. Fosdick was nowhere to be found. He left Leeds on the morning we arrived - while we were still on our way - and no one knows where he went.
[110] It's not surprising that he's always mysterious and secretive about his whereabouts. If I had the chance, I would certainly use my natural charm on him, but I doubt my father would approve of this plan, and it would undoubtedly add a lot of strain to our father-daughter relationship.
[111] The situation became even more complex with the arrival of another character – the second Mr. Clay – a tall, handsome, and charming young man named Jeremy. He had curly chestnut hair and a smirk that hinted at indifference. His name, coupled with his appropriate attire, made him seem like a protagonist straight out of a romance novel. He had recently returned from Dartmouth Harbor for various reasons.
[112] He weighed 190 pounds, had been a tail-end rower on the rowing team, and knew all the American football stars by heart. He only ate vegetables and danced like a cloud when he moved. When he arrived in Rye, he solemnly promised me over dinner that he would crush his diploma and toss it into a shredder, working alongside sweaty Italian stonemasons in his father's quarry, detonating explosives to mine the stone, and getting his hair covered in dust from the explosions. He passionately stated that he would learn to create better marble products, surpassing… his father looked proud yet somewhat skeptical.
[113] I found Jeremy to be a very charming boy. For a few days, his ambition of raising American marble appreciation was put aside as his father had him put down his work to accompany me. Jeremy had a small stable, and we spent several afternoons riding horses. My education abroad soon revealed a certain lack in one area: I had no knowledge of the art of resisting the flirting tactics of American college students.
[114] "You're just a puppy," he smoothly led our horses into a narrow valley with no room to turn, and as he suddenly grabbed my hand while walking, I growled at him.
[115] "Let's be puppies together," he smiled, leaning over on his saddle. I swung my whip and lightly tapped his nose to avoid a minor disaster.
[116] "Ouch!" he exclaimed, jumping back. "That's quite the heart rate, Petty."
[117] "I didn't!"
[118] "You did, and you liked it."
[119] "I didn't!"
[120] "Alright," he said with a knowing smirk. "I can wait." On the way home, he wore a grin that wouldn't quit.
[121] In summary, from that day on, Mr. Jeremy Clay had to ride alone, but he remained the dangerous handsome boy. In fact, I regretfully discovered that I seemed to really enjoy letting such disasters happen.
[122] The storm came upon this idyllic countryside.
[123] Like a sudden summer thunderstorm, it caught us off guard. The news came on a quiet, lazy evening. Jeremy's mood was not good, and for two hours, he kept combing his hair and I kept messing it up, teasing him.
[124] Jeremy vented his anger at his hair in a polite but distant manner, saying "Miss Sam" here and "Miss Sam" there, being overly courteous but lacking warmth. He insisted on bringing me a chair cushion, ordering the kitchen to prepare a heap of exquisite delicacies for my dinner, lighting my cigarettes, pouring me a cocktail - with a hatred of the world, on the surface, he showed polite social manners, but in his tired mind, he was boiling with the idea of self-destruction.
[125] Father came home after sunset, hurriedly, angrily, sweaty, and very agitated. He locked himself in his room, took a bath, and an hour later, he came to the veranda with a cigar. Jeremy was sadly strumming a guitar, and I was softly singing a vulgar song I learned from a café in Marseille. Luckily, I thought, Father didn't understand French. Even Jeremy, immersed in sadness, showed a look of shock. However, perhaps it was the moon and the atmosphere in the air that inspired me, for I still remember, at that time, I dreamily dreamed of walking hand in hand with Jeremy and leaving together...

[313] Chapter 4: The Fifth Letter
[314] During my first summer back in New York after being abroad, I spent a lot of time catching up on American culture. [488] After hours of investigation, the direction of the senator's murder case remained unclear until then. Now, looking back, I don't know what would have happened if we hadn't found that letter. [509] Chapter Six: The Arrival of Allen Darrow
[510] Until then, I had only felt a distant, dreamlike shadow looming over us. All the evidence in my mind was jumbled, causing me to forget the tragedy that had just occurred. But suddenly, like a knife being thrust in my back, I cut through the mist and saw it all clearly. Allen Darrow... The name meant nothing to me, it could have been John Smith or Knut Sorenson. I had never heard of him or seen him before, yet... relying on this little clue, a hunch, or intuition, or subconscious reasoning... With their conversation and pursuit, they had already convicted the unseen Allen Darrow, who had only been out of Apgar's prison for a few hours and was now in a desperate situation again.

The car sped up the steep mountain road, like a dream that was hazy and uncertain.

[515] It was hard to imagine that a building made of lifeless stone and steel could exude such a vivid aura of evil. Now, as Jeremy pressed the horn at the steel gate, I suddenly understood what it was like to be afraid of a building. Most of the prison was dark, the moon had disappeared, and a cold wind moaned. So close to the prison, yet no human voices could be heard behind the high walls, and no lights were on. I shrank in my seat, feeling my father's hand suddenly grasp mine - asking softly, "What's wrong, Petty?" His words brought me back to reality, the demon had fled, and I tried to shake off the fear.

[635] Chapter Seven: Drawing the Net Tighter

[1152] "Get to the point," Father growled. And Mr. Raine sank even lower in his chair: "All right, do you understand the predicament you're in? Hume says you rehearsed with Deo so you could act in court!"
[1153] I had a flash of insight, that guard! My intuition was right. I dared not look at Mr. Raine, who sat quietly in his chair.
[1154] "I was afraid of this," Mr. Raine finally said, his voice heavy. "It just occurred to me in Hume's office. It's my fault for not leaving myself any room to argue against the charges. I'm sorry, Mr. Collier, but since my foolishness caused this disaster, I can only make amends in the only way I know how – with money. What's your lawyer's retainer fee?"
[1155] Mr. Collier blinked and spoke slowly. "I took the case because I felt sorry for the poor guy…"
[1156] "Indeed. Tell me how much, Mr. Corryl. Perhaps this will stir up more of your courageous sympathy." The old gentleman pulled out a checkbook and prepared a pen. There was a heavy sigh from Father, and Corryl calmly held up a finger, indicating a number. I felt dizzy, and Father's mouth gaped open.
[1157] But Mr. Lane simply wrote out the check calmly and quietly placed it in front of the lawyer, "Don't spare any expense, and the bill is on me."
[1158] Corryl smiled, glancing sideways at the check on the table, and his nostrils flared slightly, "Mr. Lane, for this lawyer's fee, I would defend even the most heinous criminal."
[1159] "Yes, I was thinking –"
[1160] They continued to talk, and I could only hear a murmur of low voices. The only clear sound was the tolling of the death knell, which rang around Allen Derou's head, and it would only be silenced by a miracle.
[1161] Chapter 11: The Trial
[1162] Over the past few weeks, I found myself deeply immersed in a pit of disappointment, with a blurred vision and only a narrow gap of hope, which was so dim and ominous. The phrase "Allen Deere is doomed" echoed constantly in my mind. I drifted aimlessly around the Clary house, hoping that I would die, and Jeremy probably noticed my despondency and stopped bothering me.
[1163] I took no interest in the things around me, and my father spent all his time hanging around Mr. Rine and arguing with Mr. Collier again and again.
[1164] As the day of Allen Deere's trial approached, I noticed the old gentleman making an effort to lift his spirits and prepare for a historical battle. Occasionally, when I saw him, he was silent and closed his lips tightly. Obviously, he had put all his hopes in Mr. Collier and was busy arranging local doctors in Leeds to testify in court as experts for the defendant; breaking the silence of the prosecutor's office and achieving some success. He also sent a telegram to Dr. Martini in New York City, asking him to come to northern New York to participate in the trial.
[1165] These tasks kept him and my father busy, while I could only sit there waiting, which was a cruel torture. I tried to visit Allen Deere in the prison cell several times, but was turned away at the visiting room. If I had accompanied Mr. Collier, I could have gone there, as he was the defendant's lawyer and had the right to visit his client, but I still hesitated. For some reason, I didn't like this lawyer, and the thought of having to accompany Mr. Collier and face Deere in the cell always made me feel a little repulsive.
[1166]On the other hand, I noticed from the beginning that the case was hopeless. The selection of the jury was tedious and mechanical, with Corry persistently contesting every choice, resulting in a three-day process. I avoided looking at the pitiful old man huddled in the defendant's box, his eyes wide with anticipation, glaring at the judge, and glaring at Stryte and his entourage. I knew, and the silent old man sitting beside me knew, that Allen Darrow was looking for someone, and his silent pleas disturbed me and deepened the wrinkles on Mr. Reen's face.
[1167]We sat in the press gallery, along with Ira Clay and Jeremy, not far from us was Dr. Eula Fossett, who feigned sighs and groans to garner sympathy from the crowd. I also noticed Fanny Kather sitting quietly in the gallery, appearing to be afraid of drawing attention. Father Mull and Warden Magnus were seated behind us, and I caught a glimpse of Mr. Camichael sitting quietly to my left.
[1168]After a satisfactory jury was selected and sworn in, we settled down, waiting for the trial to continue. It didn't take long for us to realize that Stryte had the upper hand. He called several witnesses, laying out the surface facts of the crime. Following the testimony of Inspector Kane, Dr. Bull, and a few other routine witnesses, Mr. Camichael was called to the stand. His solemn and courteous demeanor initially fooled Stryte into thinking him a fool, but Mr. Camichael soon proved himself to be a cunning witness. I noticed a frown of gloom on Dr. Fossett's face.
[1169]This "secretary" played his role flawlessly. He succinctly recounted what he knew, forcing Stryte to repeatedly rephrase his questions in clearer terms, and the trial had not yet reached its core when Stryte began to lose his composure... The half-box and the hastily written signature of "Allen Darrow" were listed as evidence.
[1170]The "secretary" played his role flawlessly. He succinctly recounted what he knew, forcing Stryte to
[1171] After Magnum Opus, the warden took the witness stand and repeated his testimony about Senator Bickford's visit to Alcatraz, although most of the testimony was removed from the record due to Corrigan's strong objection, but it had a profound and indelible impact on the jury - most of the jurors were successful farmers or businessmen with gray hair.
[1172] The trial lasted for several days, and the situation was clear. After the preliminary arguments presented by Sirwit were finished, his task of proving the defendant guilty could be said to have been accomplished elegantly. From the continuous nodding of the journalists, the anxious and focused faces of the jury, I could already feel the atmosphere of the prosecution completing its task.
[1173] On the surface, Mark Collier seemed unaffected by the ominous atmosphere in the courtroom, as he calmly continued to work, and I quickly realized what was going through his mind. He, his father, and Mr. Reine had decided that the only way to make their defense effective was to simply state the details upon which their theory was based, and then draw the inevitable conclusion for the jury. I could also see that the jurors Collier had selected were quite intelligent, as during the selection process, he would quickly disqualify any potential juror who showed any signs of being dull, thus assembling a highly intelligent jury.
[1174] Mr. Collier methodically laid the groundwork, calling Mr. Carmichael to the witness stand, where he first publicly stated that he had hidden outside the house on the night of the murder, observing the masked mystery visitor, and that only one person entered or left the house during the time of the murder. Mr. Sweet questioned Mr. Carmichael's testimony aggressively, asking questions that concerned me and could have resulted in unfavorable answers; however, Mr. Carmichael calmly explained that he had not previously disclosed this because he feared losing his job – thus skillfully concealing his true mission of secretly observing the late Senator. I glanced at Dr. Betsy, whose face was as stormy as a cloudy sky, and I understood that Mr. Carmichael's private investigation work for the government would soon come to an end.
[1175] The farcical show continues, with Dr. Bauer, Cain, my father, and the local police department's experts all testifying—one by one—to build the foundation of my theory, and after Carril carefully records all the facts, he summons Allen Darrow to the witness stand.
[1176] He looked truly pitiful: terrified, constantly licking his lips, mumbling the oath, hunching over in his chair, and with only one eye that looked frightened. Corry quickly began questioning, and it was clear that Darrow had been coached, with the questioning focusing on Darrow's ten-year-old manslaughter conviction in order to block the assistant prosecutor's path and prevent unfavorable testimony against the defendant from being introduced later. For each question, Swift protested loudly, but when Corry calmly pointed out that these questions establishing the basis of the defense were unnecessary to protest, Swift's protests were overruled by the judge.
[1177] "Your Honor, ladies and gentlemen of the jury, I will prove that Senator Fosdick was killed by a right-handed person, and the defendant is a left-handed person," he said calmly.
[1178] We reached the critical point; would the jury accept the opinions of our experts? Was Swift prepared? I watched his pale face and felt my heart sink. He was waiting patiently, like a hunter, for the final showdown.
[1179] It was all over, the smoke of battle had cleared, and I sat there numbly. Our experts had messed things up, even Dr. Lane's personal physician, a renowned practitioner, could not convince the jury.
[1180] The absurd farce continued, with Dr. Bull, Kennan, my father, and the local police experts all testifying - one by one, the foundation of my theory was being built, and when Corry had meticulously recorded all the facts, he called Allen Darrow to the witness stand.
[1181] Because Swift had also hired a group of experts, who challenged the theory that "when a person becomes right-handed, he will also become left-footed," resulting in a series of lengthy and boring testimonies from doctors, ending in a stalemate, with each doctor testifying on the stand, refuting the previous one. Poor jury, they had no idea which side's opinion was correct.
[1182] Again and again, Mark Corry skillfully simplified and explained our theory; however, Swift's counterarguments overturned them one by one. Desperate, Corry called Mr. Reen, me, and my father to the witness stand, hoping that our testimony about the experiments we conducted in Derouin's cell would salvage the opinions of the experts. Swift eagerly accepted the challenge, launching a fierce counterattack in cross-examination, distorting our words and demanding the recall of a witness, a guard with a sinister expression at the detention center.
[1183] This man maliciously accused us of premeditated conspiracy in the rehearsal of the reaction of the feet and Derouin. Corry protested loudly, tugging at his sparse hair, almost coming to blows with Swift, but I knew the damage had been done. The jury swayed back to Swift's side, believing his accusations to be true — I sat there, for the next few hours, watching the pitiful Alonzo Derouin take the witness stand again, obediently using his left hand to pinch, hit, and grip; then stomping on things, first with both feet, then with his left foot, then his right foot — in various positions and performing various actions, until he was gasping for breath, frightened and angry, and very upset, having gone through all this, he seemed to prefer being convicted than enduring these torments anymore. This intensified the pessimistic and uncertain atmosphere.
[1184] On the last day of the trial, Corry delivered his closing argument, and we all knew the game was up. He had fought a tough battle and lost, and he knew it. However, he showed resilience, and I think, though he lost, he had indeed given his all for the generous lawyer's fee.
[1185] "I will tell you this," he shouted at the weary and perplexed jury, "if you sentence this man to the electric chair, it will be the most serious blow to the legal and medical authorities in the past twenty years! This case against the defendant is a clever but erroneous fabrication by the prosecution, a coincidence of fate that created all the reasonable circumstantial evidence that ensnared this poor, confused man. You have heard the testimony of the experts, and in any position, he would instinctively use his left foot to extinguish a burning piece of paper, but you know the killer used his right foot; moreover, only one person entered that room that night. How can you doubt the defendant's innocence? Mr. Swift is quite clever, but he is too clever. No matter how many experts he finds to provide opposing testimony, I will say that the defendant's main defense expert is Dr. Martini of New York, whose personal integrity, professional reputation, and profound professional knowledge are not to be defamed by the prosecution!"
[1186] "Gentlemen of the jury, no matter how despicable the surface evidence may be, no matter how cunningly the prosecutor has implanted the idea of premeditation, you cannot, in good conscience, declare this pitiful and unfortunate man guilty of a crime he physically cannot commit and sentence him to death on the electric chair!"
[1187] After six and a half hours of deliberation, Alan Deauville was found guilty of the charges against him.
[1188] Due to the controversial nature of some evidence, the jury suggested that the judge consider leniency when sentencing.
[1189] Ten days later, Alan Deauville was sentenced to life imprisonment.
[1190] Chapter Twelve: Aftermath
[1191] Corry's subsequent appeal is denied, and Allen Darrow is handcuffed by a burly deputy sheriff and sent to Aiken Prison to serve his sentence, which will not end until he dies unless he dies in prison.
[1192] We learn of Darrow's condition from Father Mullett. Following the usual procedure, Darrow is treated as a new inmate upon his return to Aiken Prison, despite his previous good record. He must go through the prison's rank system again to regain his former status; if he survives and behaves well, he may eventually gain the sympathy of the administrators and become a useful member of the iron-fisted society of the lost soul.
[1193] Day after day, week after week, time passes, but Mr. Jury's face remains etched with sadness and despair. I am surprised by his stubbornness in refusing to return to Hamlet Manor and staying at Father Mullett's house, spending his days soaking up the sun in the priest's small garden and occasionally chatting with Father Mullett and Warden Magnus, constantly inquiring about Darrow's condition.
[1194] The old gentleman seems to be waiting for something to happen, and I have kept a close eye on him, but I cannot determine what he is expecting or why he remains in Rye, possibly due to his inability to accept Darrow's conviction.
[1195] Some unrelated events occurred. With the death of Senator Vance, all opposition newspapers began to expose the graft of the Vance faction, putting Senator Vance's political position in jeopardy. The murder of Senator Vance has dispelled John Hume's previous doubts, and he has begun to openly attack Senator Vance's actions during his term, using the most nakedly defamatory tactics, with no sense of guilt about his opponent's vices. The most depraved rumors about Senator Vance's character and political career began to circulate in the city, and it is clear that many of the incriminating evidence uncovered during the investigation into Senator Vance's murder is now being used by Hume and Rufus Cotten to retaliate against the enemy camp, with great success.
[1196] However, Dr. Vesite would not easily concede defeat, and his innate talent for politics and the secret of his success were reflected in his methods of retaliation. An unimaginative politician might resort to insults to counter Hume's malicious accusations, but Dr. Vesite did not do so, maintaining dignity and silence in the face of all the defamation.
[1197] His only retort was to nominate Elijah Clay for Senator.
[1198] We remained at Clay's residence as guests, affording me the opportunity to witness the meticulous process of the whole operation. Apart from his substantial financial background, Elijah Clay had a good reputation in Tireling County. He was active in charity, a leading figure in the local business community, held a prominent position in the Rye Chamber of Commerce, and was a benevolent employer to the workers - from Dr. Vesite's perspective, the ideal candidate to counter the reform advocate John Hume.
[1199] One evening, Dr. Vesite visited and spent two hours in a private room with Elijah Clay. We were able to glimpse a hint of his intentions for the first time. When they finally emerged, Dr. Vesite was as oily and fawning as ever, and then drove away. We all stood in the hallway, relieved to see this scene.
[1200] "You'll never guess what he asked me," Mr. Clay said, his tone filled with surprise, as if even he couldn't believe it himself.
[1201] "He wants you to be his political puppet," Father says slowly, with a disapproving tone.
[1202] Mr. Clay's eyes widen: "How did you know?"
[1203] "It's simple," Father says calmly. "A scheming villain like him would certainly have that idea. What did he say?"
[1204] "He wants me to agree to run for the office of senator and accept Dr. Baxter's votes."
[1205] "Are you from that party?"
[1206] Clay blushed. "I think their ideology -"
[1207] "Dad!" Jeremy shouted. "Are you trying to get yourself in trouble?"
[1208] "Oh, of course not," Clay hastily said. "Needless to say, I refused him. However, his strict standards almost convinced me. He said that, given the current situation, it would be in the best interest of the party to have a clean and honest candidate - like me."
[1209] "Well, then," said the father, "what's the problem?"
[1210] We all stared at him in surprise.
[1211] "Damn it," the father chuckled, taking a satisfied bite of his cigar. "Clay, you got him back with his own medicine. Since we've seen through his game, you should accept the nomination!"
[1212] "But, sir—" Jeremy's voice was shocked.
[1213] "Don't worry about it, boy," the father chuckled. "Don't you want a senator for a dad? Clay, think about it. We both know we can't catch our partner doing anything, he's too smart. So, let's play along. You accept his offer, and you're in with them—understand? Maybe you can even get some written evidence, who knows? Once those clever guys get too confident, they'll do something stupid. And if you can get the evidence before the vote, you can still drop out at the last minute, leaving your supporters to clean up the mess."
[1214] "I don't like it," Jeremy muttered.
[1215] "I understand your concern," Clay frowned, looking anxious. "I don't know, sir. It seems too cunning. I—"
[1216] "Of course," the father's tone was dreamy, "It takes courage, but by exposing these scoundrels, you can bring a great change to yourself and the citizens of this county, and become a true civic hero!"
[1217] "Hmm," Clay's eyes began to shine, "Sir, I never thought of it that way! Maybe you're right. Yes, I believe you're right! I'll try it. I'll call him right now and tell him I've changed my mind!"
[1218] I couldn't help but feel a surge of opposition, what good could it do? I shook my head in confusion, unsure of my father's scheme. In my opinion, a few weeks ago, the shrewd and ambitious bearded doctor seemed to have seen through my father's intentions, suspecting that he was investigating his accounts and records at Clay Enterprises. He might have known Clay would refuse the offer to run for senator, and that my father would urge him to accept. Perhaps these reasons were too trivial, but there was one thing that mattered—something my father told me—almost from the moment we arrived, all illegal traces between the Fosters and Clay Marble Company had vanished. He seemed to be behaving innocently on the surface. Dr. Foster might have been trying to drag the honest citizen into his schemes by nominating Elias Clay, perhaps even involving him in some improper conspiracies, effectively silencing Clay forever and preventing him from exposing the anonymous partner's illegal activities.
[1219] After all, these were just my guesses, and I thought that perhaps my father knew what was best and didn't express his opinion.
[1220] "It's just Dr. Foster's old trick!" Jeremy exclaimed as Clay stood up to enter the house, "Sir, your advice is probably not appropriate."
[1221] "Jeremy." His father said with embarrassment.
[1222] "I'm sorry, Dad, but I can't keep silent. I have to tell you that if you agree, you'll get into trouble."
[1223] "Why not let me make my own decision?"
[1224] "Alright, I'll let you make your own decision," Jeremy stood up. "Dad, that's a dead end," he said ominously. "But don't blame me when it happens."
[1225] So he bid us a hasty goodnight and strode into the house.
[1226] The next morning, I found a note on my plate at breakfast.
[1227] Elijah Clay looked pale, Jeremy was gone – the note said he went to work, now to "take care of the father's business, I guess he's too busy with politics."
[1228] After the conviction of Allen Darrow, a direct consequence came to us. We kept in touch with Carmichael, who could provide some important information about Dr. Fuchs. But whether it was the federal agent's over-enthusiasm, or Dr. Fuchs' sharp eyesight behind the mask, or his testimony in the trial aroused his employer's suspicion – or all of the above – the result was that Carmichael was suddenly fired, and Dr. Fuchs did not explain the reason. One morning, Carmichael came to the Clay house, looking dejected, with a suitcase and handbag in hand, saying he was going back to Washington.
[1229] "The work is only half done," he complained. "I could have gotten all the evidence in a few more weeks. What I have now is not enough, but I got some bank deposit records, copies of canceled checks, and a long list of anonymous depositors."
[1230] Before leaving, Carmichael promised us that once he handed over his findings to his superiors in the federal government, he would take the necessary legal action to punish the political power in Tire County. At that time, my father and I thought that Dr. Fuchs was one step ahead of us, and our spy had been forced to leave the enemy's headquarters, and the source of information was cut off.
[1231] I was deeply depressed by the unpleasant situation, my father was constantly angry, Elijah Clay was busy with his campaign, and Jeremy was mining in his father's mine with explosives, ignoring the danger of death and disability. Suddenly, I had an idea that if McMichael left, someone should take his place, and I thought I would try.
[1232] The more I thought about it, the better I felt about the idea. I was sure that Dr. Fusite knew the real mission of Father's coming to Lizzie, and with my innocent appearance, I couldn't see why he wouldn't fall into the trap of the beauty trap like many other smarter villains.
[1233] So, I started approaching this bearded gentleman without my father's knowledge. My first move was to choose a day to meet him - oh, completely coincidentally!
[1234] "Miss Sam!" he exclaimed, examining me with a connoisseur's eager gaze - I had dressed up for this meeting, showing off my advantages, "What a pleasant surprise! I was planning to visit you."
[1235] "Really?" I teased.
[1236] "Oh, I know I've been too neglectful," he smiled, licking his lips, "but - now I want to make amends! Miss, will you have lunch with me?"
[1237] I pretended to be coy: "Dr. Bustard! You're quite possessive, aren't you?"
[1238] His eyes sparkled, and he stroked his short beard. "Far more than you can imagine," he said in a low, intimate tone, taking my hand and giving it a gentle squeeze. "My car is here."
[1239] So, I sighed and let him help me into the car. I saw him give a wink to the gruff-looking driver, Louis, and then drive to the Highway Inn - the same inn where my father and I had met Mr. Carmichael a few weeks ago. I believe the innkeeper recognized me, as he gave us a sly look and led us to a private dining room with great deference.
[1240] I was relieved that I didn't have to play the part of a Victorian novel heroine, defending my honor. Dr. Bustard turned out to be a charming host, and my opinion of him improved somewhat. He was not rude, and I assume he treated me as a young, fresh potential prey, not wanting to scare me off by being too eager. He served me a delicious lunch and a fine wine, gave my hand a squeeze across the table, and then sent me home without saying a word out of place.
[1241] I played the part of the flustered young lady, eagerly awaiting his next move. I did not underestimate my "lover," for a few days later, he called to invite me to the city's theater—a company was staging a play "Candide," and he thought I would enjoy it. I had seen "Candide" six times already—it seemed that every man who courted me thought this play by George Bernard Shaw was the prelude to a romantic interlude. Nevertheless, I pouted playfully, "Oh, doctor, I've never seen this play! I've heard it's very moving!"
[1242] He chuckled softly, agreeing to pick me up the next evening. The play was merely passable, but my companion was flawless. Many people attended, all the most prominent figures in Ritz, the ladies dressed in sparkling jewels, the gentlemen with flabby red chins and politician's cunning eyes. Dr. Fuchs hovered around me, then casually suggested that "everyone" go to his house for a cocktail. Ha! I was very clever, and I had anticipated this—I feigned confusion. Is this appropriate? I mean—he laughed heartily, of course it is! Why, dear, your father wouldn't object—I sighed, putting on an expression of a silly schoolgirl doing something very, very naughty.
[1243] However, this night was not without danger. As most people gradually dispersed on the way, only two of us remained when Dr. Fuchs and I arrived at his large, dim house—him and me. I admit that I was somewhat uneasy when he opened the front door and I stepped into the house where a corpse had lain before, but I was more afraid of the danger that lay ahead than the danger that had already passed. As we passed through the senator's study, I noticed that everything had been rearranged, and all traces of the crime had been erased, and I breathed a sigh of relief.
[1244] The highlight of my visit was to lower Dr. Fuchs' guard and pique his interest. He persuaded me and found various excuses to give me cocktails, but I had been to drinking school, and controlling my alcohol intake was a mandatory course, so he must have been surprised at how little I drank and how well I pretended to be drunk. Thus, he shed his gentleman's facade and resumed his true self. He helped me to a recliner and began to caress me with great skill. I had to be both a graceful dancer and a J. R. R. Tolkien-like performer to prevent myself from being taken advantage of or exposed. Although I managed to extricate myself from his embrace, I was still pleased: not only could I refuse his advances, but I also kept his interest piqued. It was evident that he was looking forward to savoring this delectable dish, and I surmised that half of his pleasure came from the anticipation.
[1245] Since I had broken down his defenses, I took advantage of the situation and pushed forward.
[1246] I have been visiting Fudge's den more frequently, and indeed, the intensity of his advances has been proportionate. For a month since Alan Dower's imprisonment, this risky lifestyle has persisted, and during this adventurous month, I have had to face my father's occasional questioning and Jeremy's possessive rage. The boy was furious when he didn't like my explanation of meeting a "friend" in the city, so he secretly followed me, making me feel like an eel in water, constantly evading him before finally shaking him off.
[1247] I still remember it was a Wednesday evening, and the opportunity finally came. I arrived at Mr. Fudges' house earlier than our scheduled time, and when I stepped onto the floor of his private study next to his examination room, I found him studying something – a very special something – placed on his desk. He looked up when he saw me, muttered a curse under his breath, and then quickly put on a smile while swiftly putting the item away in an upper drawer. I did my best to hide my surprise. It was – ah, too unbelievable! Yet I saw it with my own eyes. Finally, it appeared, incredible, finally it appeared.
[1248] When I stepped out of the house that night, I was shaking with excitement. His usual attempts at flirting were half-hearted, so my resistance didn't have to be as difficult as usual. Why? There was no doubt that his mind was preoccupied with the object in the top drawer of his desk.
[1249] Therefore, I did not proceed to the parking spot on the lane but instead crept around the side of the house to come to the window of Mr. Baskerville's study. If, up until now, all my previous visits had been unsuccessful – this time, perhaps, I might obtain some deadly documents – I believed this opportunity would yield far more than I could ever have imagined. My goal was not the documents, but something more important, something that made my mouth water and my throat dry, and my heart beat so loudly that I feared Dr. Baskerville could hear it through the wall.
[1250] I pulled my skirt up to my knees, grabbed hold of a sturdy grapevine, and climbed to a spot where I could see into the study, silently thanking the gods for the moonless night. My gaze swept over the windowsill and into the room, where Fuchsia was at her desk, and I almost let out a triumphant shriek, just as I had expected!
[1251] He sat there, his thin face twisted in anger, his short beard bristling, holding something tightly in his hand, almost crushing it. What was it? A letter - no, a note! He snatched it off the desk in front of him, his face contorted with horror, reading it aloud, terrifyingly, so loud that I lost my balance on the grapevine and fell to the ground with a thud loud enough to wake the dead in the grave.
[1252] He must have jumped away from the chair and rushed to the window. All I knew was that I was lying on the ground, looking up at his face appearing in the window, and I was so terrified that I became paralyzed, unable to move. His face was as black as the night, and I could only see his lips twisting and cursing, and pounding on the window, almost breaking it.
[1253] Fear gave me new strength, and I scrambled up, running down the path like the wind, faintly hearing him banging down the driveway, chasing me from behind.
[1254] He yelled, "Louis, catch her, Louis!" The driver appeared out of the darkness, grinning, reaching out, and I stumbled towards him, almost fainting, his iron grip on me was swift.
[1255] Fuchs gasped for breath, clutching my arm tightly, causing me to scream in pain. "You're a spy, it turns out!" he muttered, staring at me in disbelief, "You almost scared me, you little devil." He turned to the driver and said curtly, "Leave, Louis."
[1256] The driver replied, "Yes, sir." He disappeared into the darkness, still wearing that cold smile.
[1257] I was paralyzed with fear, huddled in Dr. Baskerville's grasp, dizzy and terrified, my heart pounding and nausea rising. I remember him shaking me violently, whispering vile things in my ear. I stole a glance at him, his eyes bulging with passion, flashing with the madness of murder...
[1258] I can't remember what happened next, whether I broke free from his grasp or he let me go willingly. All I remember is stumbling along the asphalt road, my evening gown tripping me up, with Dr. Baskerville's fingers leaving red marks on my arm.
[1259] After a while, I stopped and leaned against a black tree to rest. The cool breeze cooled my flushed cheeks, and I shamelessly shed bitter tears, suddenly missing my father. Detective! I sobbed, feeling that I should be sitting by the fireplace knitting a sweater... Then I heard the sound of a car approaching, slowly along the road.
[1260] I hugged the tree, holding my breath, once again stiff with terror. Was it Dr. Baskerville coming after me to fulfill the terrible threat in his eyes?
[1261] The headlights rounded the bend and entered my line of sight, the car moving slowly, the driver seeming hesitant... Then I ran out into the middle of the road, waving my arms wildly and screaming, "Jeremy, oh, dear Jeremy! I'm here!"
[1262] For the first time, I thanked God for creating a loyal lover, and Jeremy jumped out of the car and hugged me tightly. I let him kiss me, happy to see his familiar, kind face. He wiped away my tears and helped me into the car, sitting next to him.
[1263] He was scared too, so he didn't ask me any questions, which I was even more grateful for. However, I guess he had been following me all night, watching me enter Dr. Baskerville's house and waiting for me outside on the road. He heard the commotion in the yard, running towards the driveway when I had already escaped, and Dr. Baskerville had returned to the house.
[1264] "Jeremy, what did you just do?" I clung to his broad shoulder, trembling, and asked.
[1265] He released the steering wheel with his right hand, painfully sucking on his finger joints: "I punched him," he said briefly, "Just got lucky. Then some freak came along, probably his driver, and we had a little fight. Not for long. I was lucky—the guy was a beast."

[1292] Chapter 13: The Death of One Man
[1293] It was only weeks later that I learned how a person who had no meaning to me or anyone else involved in the case, including the Derou and Fuchs brothers and Fanny Kather, had died that night. However, despite the insignificance of his life and the tragic nature of his death, his death affected not only Derou, Dr. Fuchs, Fanny Kather, but also others. If it hadn't been for his death, some facts would have remained in the dark and would never have been clarified.
[1294] The old gentleman later told me that during the time he spent waiting hopelessly at Father Mull's house, he heard the news that a certain Scacci was about to be executed. Scacci was a member of a criminal gang, lived by violence, and died by violence, and his disappearance would be a blessing for others. During that time, Mr. Lane was bored to death and perhaps, as a normally gentle man who had grown accustomed to a peaceful life, he couldn't help but be curious. [1295] They had already discussed the matter of electrocution, so the elderly gentleman was somewhat familiar with it. "Discipline is always strict in the prison," the warden said, "It's necessary. However, the process of capital punishment is very cruel. Of course, the death row inmate lives in an isolated cell, but the rumors in the prison spread much faster than you can imagine, and other prisoners are obviously very sensitive to the so-called 'House of Death' in their slang. Therefore, during the execution of the death penalty, we will particularly strengthen security because during this short period, the whole prison will be in a state of hysterical violence, and anything can happen. In fact, we really need to be careful."
[1296] "I wouldn't envy your job."
[1297] "You certainly wouldn't," Magnus sighed. "Anyway, I always have the same group of people on duty during executions—of course, occasionally someone is unable to work due to illness or other reasons, and we have to find a replacement, but so far, there have been no such cases."
[1298] "Why is that?"
[1299] "Because," the warden said sternly, "I want the execution to be carried out by experienced people. You never know what might happen, so we select seven people from the guards who work the night shift to handle this task, as well as two prison physicians. In fact, I can say with pride that this method is quite scientific and has never caused any trouble, as these guards have been carefully chosen and the shifts are fixed—for example, I would never switch someone from the day shift to the night shift. They are all very responsible and know how to handle emergencies."
[1300] "Do you want to be a witness to Scacci's execution?" Magnus looked sharply at Mr. Raine. The elderly gentleman nodded. "Are you sure? You know, it's not a pleasant thing, and Scacci is not the kind of person who faces death with a smile."

[1307] So, while I was horrified when I visited Dr. Fossett on Thursday evening, Mr. Rinehart and Father Mool were waiting inside the prison's massive stone walls. Father Mool had left early to prepare for the last rites; Mr. Rinehart arrived alone at around eleven o'clock, accompanied by a guard, and entered the execution chamber, or "the house of death," a low-lying building in the corner of the prison, almost like a prison within a prison. The atmosphere of the building was eerie and morbid, and Mr. Rinehart felt his nerves tense up.
[1308] Naturally, his attention was immediately drawn to the heavy and ugly death weapon squatting in the room. Surprisingly, he found it to be smaller than he had expected and not as terrifying as he had imagined. The straps hung loosely from the back, arms, and legs of the chair, and a device on top resembled a football helmet. At the moment, everything looked harmless and bizarrely unreal.
[1309] He sat on the hard bench, surveying the surroundings, and noticed that the other eleven witnesses had already taken their seats. [1310] There was a small door on one side of the room, which he knew led to the morgue. [1311] There was another small door on the opposite side of the long bench, which he knew led to a corridor that the condemned man would walk down on his last journey.
[1312] The door was open, and a solemn group of people walked in, their footsteps echoing on the hard floor. In addition, there were three prison guards – wearing blue uniforms and wearing cold expressions. At this point, the old gentleman noticed a recessed alcove in the corner of the room, where a tall, middle-aged man stood. He was checking some electronic equipment in the alcove, his face serious, slow, and expressionless, almost stupid. The executioner! Until now, Philo Vance had not realized the cruel meaning of all this, and his heart began to race, and he felt a tightness in his throat, making it difficult to breathe. The room was no longer unreal; it had an evil aura, as if there were living creatures lurking within.
[1313] He glanced at his watch and saw that it was 11:06.

[1315]

[1567] Chapter 17: Playing the Heroine

[1592] We looked at each other for a long time, like two frightened accomplices.
[1593] Jeremy gritted his teeth and said briefly, "Let's go, act natural, let's go for a walk in the forest."
[1594] He smiled at me, helping me out of the convertible, holding my hand, squeezing it to reassure me, and then leading me along the path to the forest, leaning down to whisper in my ear. For the onlookers, it was like a young man flirting with his girlfriend. I giggled, gazing deeply into his eyes, but my mind was in chaos. What we were about to do was so terrifying, and I wasn't sure if the barely breathing Allen Darrow would have a chance to escape the electric chair's fate...
[1595] After what seemed like an endless stretch of road, we finally entered the forest, cool tree shadows overhead, and the scent of trees filled our nostrils. The world seemed far away, even the occasional sound of the mine explosion seemed distant and unreal. We abandoned our silly lover act, running wildly, Jeremy leading the way, moving swiftly like an Indian, and I panted behind him.
[1596] Suddenly, I bumped into him, and he stopped, his young, open face showing a look of alarm. Alarm, fear, and then despair.
[1597] Then I heard it too, the sound of alarms and barking dogs.
[1598] "Oh no," he whispered, "it's slim hope, Petty, they've tracked him down by scent."
[1599] "Too late," I murmured, my heart tightening, holding his arm, and he shook me violently, shaking my teeth. "Damn it, don't give me the weak woman act now!"
[1600] "Come on, maybe it's not too late," he snapped, "let's go."
[1601] He turned and rushed down the dim path into the forest, and I hurried after him, bewildered and confused, and annoyed with him. He dared to shake me like that? He dared to scold me?
[1602] He stopped abruptly, covering my mouth with his hand, and then bent down, crawling on all fours through a patch of dusty shrubs, occasionally pulling me back, and I bit my lips to keep from crying, my skirt tearing on the thorns of the trees, and my fingers cut, and then I forgot the pain, and before me was a small clearing in the forest.
[1603] Too late! In front of us, a dilapidated shack swayed precariously, and on the other side of the clearing, the sound of hounds barking furiously could be heard.
[1604] For a moment, the clearing seemed calm and empty, but the peace was soon shattered as uniformed guards aimed their rifles at the shack, and the hounds - those hideous beasts - leaped lightning-fast onto the shack's door, clawing and jumping, emitting terrible growls...
[1605] Three people ran forward, grabbed the leashes, and pulled the dogs back.
[1606] We stood in silence and despair.
[1607] A red flash accompanied by a crack of gunfire erupted from one of the shack's small windows, and I saw a revolver barrel disappear inside, followed by a dripping-jawed hound, which suddenly contorted and fell dead.
[1608] "Don't come any closer!" a shrill, hysterical voice - it was Allen Darrow - "Don't come any closer! Or you'll end up like that mutt. You won't catch me alive. I tell you, don't come any closer!"
[1609] Crawling on my knees, a mad idea took hold of me. I didn't care anymore. I believed Darrow when he said he would do it, and he might really commit a murder. But there was an opportunity, a very slim and insane chance...
[1610] Jeremy dragged me back again: "Good heavens, Petty, what are you doing?" he whispered, and I struggled, his mouth agape... As we fought, changes occurred on the clearing, and I saw Magnum the warden crouching among a group of guards, who retreated behind bushes and trees, some of them gradually approaching our hiding place, every armed guard's eyes filled with the burning desire to hunt...
[1611] The jailor stepped into the open: "Deere," he called calmly, "don't be a fool. The hut is surrounded, and we will catch you. We don't want to kill you…"
[1612] Bang! As if in a dream, I saw a red streak appear magically on the jailor's exposed right arm, and blood began to drip onto the dry ground. Deere fired again. A guard jumped out of the bushes, dragging the dazed jailor back.
[1613] I struggled to break free from Jeremy's grip, my heart pounding, and ran into the open. Suddenly, the universe stopped spinning, and I found everything suddenly silent, as if the jailor, guards, dogs, and even Deere himself were stunned by my reckless suicide attempt. But I was excited and in a half-mad state of mind due to the terrible idea in my head, and I couldn't control my muscles anymore.
[1614] I prayed silently that Jeremy wouldn't jump out after me, and almost at the same moment, I saw three guards crawling behind him and pouncing on him, struggling fiercely.
[1615] I looked up and heard my own clear and crisp voice saying, "Allen Deere, let me in. You know who I am. I'm Percy Sam. Let me in. I need to talk to you." As I casually walked straight towards the hut.
[1616] My mind went blank, and I couldn't think. If Deo shot me out of fear, I would never know how I died.
[1617] A piercing sound pierced my eardrums: "Back off, the rest of you! She's in my hands, anyone who moves, I'll kill her! Back off!"
[1618] So I walked to the door, and it opened. I stepped into the dim shadows, smelling the damp odor inside. The door slammed shut behind me, and I leaned against it, dizzy with fear and trembling like an old woman with chills…
[1619] The poor creature looked terrible—dirty, messy, with a scruffy beard, ugly and repulsive, and submissive like Quasi. However, his eyes were determined, the calm and resolve of a brave man facing an inevitable death. He had a smoking revolver in his left hand.

Father and Mr. Renn rushed over, and I remember sitting in Mr. Hume's office, listening to the pitiful confession of poor Aaron Deavereaux. I also remember him huddled in the chair, twisting his withered, wrinkled head from my face to Mr. Renn's, then to my father's. I was exhausted, dazed, and Mr. Renn looked tragic. An hour before entering Mr. Hume's office, I had told Mr. Renn what I had promised Deavereaux in the shed, and I will never forget what Mr. Renn said and how he looked at that moment.

[1628] Then I realized what I had done. Once again, I gave hope to this man, and once again…
[1629] He answered the questions. No, he didn't kill Dr. Fuchs. He didn't even enter the house… Mr. Hume took out the revolver Deavereaux had used in the shed from the drawer.

[1632] "I—yes, I saw it, but at the time I thought he was dead…"
[1633] "Dare, are you sure he's dead?"
[1634] "Yes, yes, sir, I'm sure!"

He screamed that he had never seen the note, he had never seen Dr. Fuchs's handwritten signature letter; and the letter written in pencil with capital letters, signed "Allen Dare," he had never written.
[1636] "Did you receive any letters from Dr. Veskit during your last days in prison?" the old gentleman asked quickly.

[1640] What followed was truly terrifying. John Hume once again chose the simple procedure: to have the assistant district attorney prosecute the case. Since D'Artagnan's first-degree murder charge was undeniable, and the prosecution was remarkably efficient, the trial began at lightning speed. The only difference was that this time, the people of Leeds could not watch, they wanted justice to be served to this man. The second murder charge against D'Artagnan infuriated the people, and it took extraordinary courage to try to keep him from returning to the prison with its guarded and shadowy walls.
[1641] It was incomprehensible that Mark Corry refused Mr. Reen's legal fees, his smug, pudgy face enigmatic, refusing to take up the case again for a case without hope.
