[1643] "Allen Darrow... sentenced to death and to be executed within one week, starting on X month X…"
[1644] Two deputy sheriffs cuffed Allen Darrow and, surrounded by armed guards, he was escorted to the Aroostook Prison. The silence of the death row cell was like the frozen earth under a winter tombstone, falling upon him.
[1645] Chapter 18: The Dark Hour
[1646] We drifted on the calm sea, praying for a breeze, but only the relentless sun shone down. We were all nearly exhausted - tired of waiting for the wind to rise, tired of fighting, tired of seeking a way out.
[1647] Father and Elijah Clayton reconciled their differences, and we had no desire to argue, so we stayed at their house. We only went back to sleep at night, spending little time there otherwise. Father constantly roamed the city like a ghost, while I spent most of my time on the hill at Father Mulholland's house, perhaps out of some sense of guilt, hoping to be closer to the condemned man. Father Mulholland visited Alonzo Drew every day, but for some reason, he wouldn't reveal Drew's condition. From the pain on Father Mulholland's face, I could guess that Drew must have cursed us all, but it was too late.
[1648] Everything was settled. There were some small incidents. I learned that Mr. Rine had secretly visited Alonzo Drew while he was waiting in the detention cell for his conviction and sentencing. I don't know what they talked about, but it must have been extraordinary, as since then, the old gentleman's face has been plagued by a look of fear.
[1649] I once asked about the content of their conversation, and he remained silent for a long time before saying, "He refused to tell me what Hijaz meant." He said nothing else.
[1650] There was another time when he suddenly disappeared, and we searched for him for four hours, almost going crazy, and then he reappeared quietly, sitting back in the rocking chair on Father Mulholland's porch as if he had never left. He sat there tiredly and indifferently, lost in his troubled thoughts. Later, I found out that he had gone to see Rufus Cotton to resolve some doubts in his theory. At the time, I didn't understand what he hoped to gain from this mysterious visit, but it was clear that his purpose had failed.
[1651] There was another time when, after hours of silence, he suddenly jumped up and called for Dromio to drive the car, then rushed off to Ritz, disappearing in a cloud of dust. They returned shortly, and a few hours later, a messenger came up the hill on a bicycle with a telegram. Mr. Rine's serpent-like eyes eagerly read it and then dropped it on my knees.
[1652] The federal detective is currently out in the Midwest. Please maintain absolute secrecy.
[1653] The telegram was signed by a high-ranking official in the Department of Justice. I believe that Mr. Rine was holding out a glimmer of hope to consult with Mr. Campbell, but it was clear that it was to no avail.
[1654] Of course, the old gentleman was the true victim. It's hard to believe that the same man who was so full of excitement and joy a few weeks ago, accompanying us to Richmond, is now the same Mr. Rine. Something seemed to have been drained from him, leaving only a breath, and he had returned to being the sickly old man. Except for occasionally disappearing, he and Father Mure sat in silence, wasting endless empty time, pondering strange thoughts known only to God.
[1655] Time passed slowly, and then slipped away unnoticed, with one quiet day following another. But one morning, I woke up groggily, suddenly remembering that it was Friday, and my whole body stiffened in fear. By law, the Magnus warden must decide on Allen Darrow's execution date within a week starting next week. However, it was just a formality, as it was the custom at the Apgun Prison to execute death sentences on Tuesday evenings. Therefore, unless a miracle occurs, Allen Darrow will be nothing but a charred corpse in two weeks... The thought left me bewildered, and I wanted to find someone to help, appeal to the authorities, and do everything possible to save the poor man trapped behind the walls. But who should I go to?
[1656] That afternoon, I dropped by Father Mule's house as usual and found my father there, discussing with Mr. Rine and the priest. I quietly sat down, closed my eyes, and then opened them again.
[1657] Mr. Rine said, "Inspector, it seems there is no hope. I'm going to Albany to find Bruno."
[1658] The conflict between friendship and duty is a common theme in drama.
[1659] If it were not for the seriousness of the situation, this conflict would have been quite entertaining.
[1660] Father and I were overjoyed to have the opportunity to take action, and we insisted on accompanying the old gentleman to Albany, and he seemed quite willing. Dromio drove tirelessly like a Spartan warrior, but when we finally arrived at the New York State capital on the hill, Father and I were exhausted. However, Mr. Rine did not listen to any suggestions of delay. He had already called Rine before arriving in Rye and Governor Bruno was waiting for us. So Dromio drove non-stop, without stopping for a snack or rest, straight to the Governor's Mansion.
[1661] We met the governor in his office in the State Capitol building - a bald, brown-haired, stern-eyed, sturdy old Bruno. He warmly welcomed us, ordered a secretary to bring him a sandwich, and cheerfully bantered with my father and Mr. Reen... but his eyes remained serious and alert, while his mouth smiled.
[1662] "Now," he said, after we had comfortably eaten and rested, "Mr. Reen, what brings you to Albany?"
[1663] "The case of Allen Darrow," the old gentleman replied calmly.
[1664] "I had guessed as much," Mr. Bruno quickly tapped on his desk, "tell me everything."
[1665] So the old gentleman told him, in calm, objective, and concise words, without creating any preconceived impressions. He patiently explained why Allen Darrow could not have killed the first victim, Senator Fuchs. Mr. Bruno listened with a furrowed brow, showing no emotion.
[1666] "So," Reen concluded, "considering these facts, it is indeed questionable whether O'Farrell is guilty. Governor, we came here to ask you to postpone the execution date."
[1667] Governor Bruno opened his eyes. "Reen, your analysis is still as impressive as ever, and in most cases, I would say it is correct. But - there is no evidence."
[1668] "Listen, Bruno," my father bellowed, "I know you're in a tough spot, but you've got to trust yourself! You know me too well! You have to postpone the execution date!"
[1669] Governor Bruno sighed. "This is the most difficult task I have faced since taking office, Sam and Reen. I am merely a tool of the law. Yes, I have sworn to uphold the law, but our legal system operates on facts, and you have no facts, brother, no 'facts.' It's all theory - perfect, resonant theory, but that's all it is. I cannot interfere with the execution after a jury has found guilt and a judge has pronounced a death sentence, unless I am certain the death row inmate is innocent based on evidence and morality. Give me evidence, evidence!"
[1670] The scene fell into an awkward silence, and I sat uneasily in my chair, feeling a blank of helplessness in my heart. Then Reen stood up, looking tall and imposing, with the lines of exhaustion and pallor etched on his weary face. "Bruno, I came here not only with the theory of Allen O'Farrell's innocence, but also with certain destructive inferences drawn from those two shocking and clear murders. However - as you say - reasoning is not a conclusion unless supported by evidence, and I have no evidence."
[1671] The father's eyes almost popped out, and he exclaimed, "So, you mean you 'know'?"
[1672] Mr. Rain impatiently made a strange gesture: "I almost know everything, not everything, but pretty close. He leaned over the governor's desk, staring intently into Bruno's eyes, "Bruno, in the past, you've always trusted me in difficult situations. Why won't you trust me this time?"
[1673] Governor Bruno lowered his eyes: "Dear Mr. Rain... I can't."
[1674] "Very well, then," the old gentleman straightened up, "I'll be more explicit. My deductions haven't yet pointed out who the murderer of Senator and Dr. Fuchs is, but, Bruno, my analysis has led me to a stage very close to the truth. I can be very precise in saying that the killer can only be one of the three specific individuals."
[1675] The father and I stared at him blankly. One-third! This statement seemed too surprising and impossible. I had already narrowed down the possible range to a specific number, but - three! I didn't understand how, based on the facts known so far, the pool of suspects could be reduced to such a small number.
[1676] The governor murmured: "And Allen Darrow is not one of the three?"
[1677] "No."
[1678] Mr. Lane's answer was very definitive, and I saw Mr. Bruno's worried eyes flicker.
[1679] "Trust me, give me time, 'time', understand? That's all I need and want. Time will reveal... the puzzle is still missing a piece, a very important piece, and I need to spend time to find it."
[1680] "Perhaps that piece doesn't exist," Bruno grumbled, "What if everything is in vain? Do you understand my position?"
[1681] "Then I'll concede. But unless I'm sure it doesn't exist, you have no moral right to dictate Darrow's fate and sentence him to death for a crime he didn't commit."
[1682] Mr. Bruno, the governor, looked up sharply: "Well, then," his lips moved rapidly, "I'll do this much for you. If you haven't found the final key by the time of execution, I'll postpone it by a week."
[1683] "Oh," Mr. Raine said, "thank you, Bruno. You're too kind. It's the first ray of sunshine in weeks of gloom. Sam, Percy – let's go back!"

[1685] The old gentleman looked up sharply: "What is it?"
[1686] "You're not the only one asking for the cancellation of Allen DeO's execution."
[1687] "So?"
[1688] "There's someone from Riz City –"
[1689] "You mean," Mr. Rain said, his eyes sparkling with excitement, "Governor Bruno, there's someone we know, and involved in this case, who came before us to ask you to postpone?"
[1690] "Not to postpone," the governor whispered, "to pardon. She came two days ago, although she didn't tell me why –"
[1691] "Who is she?" We all stood stunned, exclaiming in unison.
[1692] "Fanny Kather."
[1693] Mr. Raine stared blankly at the oil painting above the governor's head: "Fanny Kather. Well, I see. I have -" he slammed his fist on the desk, "of course, of course! How could I be so blind, so stupid! She didn't explain why she wants you to pardon her, did she?" He strode across the carpet towards us, grabbing our arms and squeezing them until they hurt: "Percy, Chief Inspector - go back to Leeds! Tell you, there's hope!"
[1694] Chapter 19: All-Out Chase
[1695] The journey back to Leeds was strange, with the weather turning colder and Mr. Raine wrapped in his thick coat, his eyes burning with a fierce flame. I could feel his strong will driving the car's wheels, and he remained silent, occasionally lifting his head to order Dromio to drive faster.
[1696] However, humans cannot resist the physiological needs of eating and sleeping, and we were forced to stop for the night. We resumed our journey the next day, and by noon, we finally arrived in Leeds.
[1697] The streets seemed to be in an unexpected commotion, newsboys shouting headlines, and the front page featured a striking title. I suddenly heard a few words from a news vendor: Fanny Kater!
[1698] "Stop!" I shouted to Dromio, "Something big has happened."
[1699] Before my father and Mr. Reen could react, I jumped out of the car, threw a copper to the newsboy, and grabbed a newspaper.
[1700] "I found it!" I screamed, quickly climbing back into the car, "Look!"
[1701] The report in the Leeds Observer said that Fanny Kates "has been notorious for years and has been ordered arrested by the district attorney John Hume and will be charged with...". A long list of offenses followed: human trafficking, drug trafficking, and other vile deeds. According to the report, Hume seemed to have made good use of the documents found during the search of the Westcott house during the investigation of the first murder case. Fanny Kates' various "hideouts" were raided, private bribes were exposed, and all sorts of the most vicious rumors were spread, and it was clear that many prominent figures in Leeds society, business leaders, and politicians were directly involved.
[1702] Kates' bail was set at $25,000, and we noticed that she quickly secured her release and regained her freedom, awaiting trial.
[1703] "This is big news," Mr. Lane said thoughtfully. "Lucky, Chief, I can't say how lucky, our friend Fanny Kates is in a tough spot now, perhaps...". He didn't seem to care about the woman's arrest and prosecution, her downfall. "This kind of person will do everything to clear themselves... Drive to the office of the district attorney Hume, Dr. Moo."
[1704] Hume was sitting at his desk, leisurely smoking a cigar, and he greeted us with great pleasure. Where is the woman now? She has been bailed. Where is her headquarters? He chuckled and gave us an address.
[1705] We arrived at the location – a large house in a remote area of the city, which had obviously been searched by the police. It was opulent, lavish, and gaudy, decorated with a large number of enthusiastic but dubiously artistic nude paintings. She was not there, and since her release from jail, she had not returned.
[1706] We frantically searched for her, falling into panic once again. Three hours later, we stood silently and despondently, gazing at each other: the woman had vanished, and we could not find her anywhere.
[1707] Could she have fled bail and left the state—perhaps the country? Given the serious charges against her, this was a distinct possibility. We watched in frustration as the elderly gentleman coldly and efficiently notified John Hume and the police, issuing a warrant for Katherine's arrest, searching all of her usual haunts, deploying undercover detectives to find her, and placing surveillance on train stations. However, all efforts proved futile, and the woman had disappeared.
[1708] "Damn it," John Hume muttered, exhaustedly sitting in his private office, waiting for updates. "We were planning to file charges against her in three weeks, that is, two weeks after next Thursday."
[1709] We all groaned, even with Governor Bruno's decision to postpone the execution of the death penalty, Fanny Katherine would not appear until the day after the execution of Allen Darrow—if she appeared at all.
[1710] The days that followed were agonizing, and a week passed. Friday… We did not give up our search. Mr. Lane was full of energy, coordinating with the police, arranging for local radio stations to broadcast constant appeals and calls for information. Everyone who had any connection with her was under surveillance, and her associates—including women, lawyers, henchmen, and members of the Leeds underworld—were questioned at her headquarters.
[1711] Saturday, Sunday, Monday… On Tuesday, we learned from Father Mueller and the newspapers that Warden Magnus had officially announced the execution time as Tuesday evening at 11:05 p.m.
[1712] Wednesday… Fanny Kittredge remained nowhere to be found, telegrams were sent to all European shipping lines, but no female passenger resembling Kittredge was aboard any ship.
[1713] Wednesday morning… We felt like we were living in a dream, barely able to eat and only speaking in whispers. Father Mueller had not changed his clothes for forty-eight hours, Mr. Reen's cheeks were as pale as a corpse, and his eyes burned with a melancholic fire. Desperate, we attempted to visit Allen Darrow at Aylesbury Prison and Dartmoor, but were denied entry due to strict prison regulations. However, we managed to obtain some information: Darrow was remarkably calm, almost detached, no longer cursing us, and in fact, he seemed to have forgotten our existence. As the execution time drew nearer, he stepped into his cell with a twisted gait, and the horrors he would face became increasingly clear in our minds; however, Father Mueller's eyes welled with tears as he smiled and told us, "He held fast to his faith." Poor Father Mueller! Allen Darrow held not to spiritual faith, but to a more worldly hope. My intuition told me that Mr. Reen somehow conveyed a message to him, telling him that he would not die that night.
[1714] Wednesday, a day of terror and surprise. We hardly touched our breakfast. Father Mueller had left to visit the death row cell in the prison courtyard and then rushed back to his upstairs bedroom to rest. When he reappeared with the prayer book in hand, he seemed much calmer.
[1715] Naturally, we all gathered at Father Mueller's house that day. I dimly remember that Jeremy was also there, his young face hanging with humility, pacing heavily outside the door, chain-smoking. Once I went out to find him, he told me that his father had done a terrible thing, that Warden Elias Clay had invited him to serve as a witness to the execution, and — Jeremy lamented — he had accepted. I couldn't think of what to say… So a day passed, Mr. Reen's face was taut, wrinkles appearing, and he had not slept for two nights, his relentless anguish etched deeply into his face.
[1716] For some reason, the whole thing felt like a family gathering outside the room of a dying patient, with no one saying anything, and if someone spoke, it was in a hushed voice.
[1717] Occasionally, someone would step out onto the porch and silently gaze at the gray prison walls. I wondered why we all seemed to care so much about this poor man's death, who meant nothing to us personally. However, in a certain sense, he captivated us - he may have symbolized some abstract meaning.
[1718] Around 11 am on Wednesday, Mr. Lane received a messenger from the prosecutor's office in Leeds with the final report. All efforts had been in vain, and no trace of Fanny Kaiser or her whereabouts had been found.
[1719] The elderly gentleman straightened his shoulders: "There's only one thing left to do," he said quietly, "remind Bruno to postpone the execution until we find Fanny Kaiser...”
[1720] The doorbell rang, and we immediately sensed that something had happened. Father Mueller rushed to the door, and we heard his choked sobs of joy.
[1721] We stood there dumbfounded, staring at the figure standing by the door to the living room.
[1722] It was Fanny Kather, who seemed to have come back from the dead.
[1723] Chapter 20: Z's Tragedy
[1724] The calm, cigar-smoking, commanding Amazonian warrior who used to face John Hume was nowhere to be seen. Instead, a completely different person stood before us. Her once deep red hair was stained pink and gray; her masculine clothes were dirty and wrinkled, with a few tears in them; the powder on her cheeks and lips had sagged, and her eyes – they shone with raw fear.
[1725] She was a frightened old woman.
[1726] We rushed forward to help her, half-dragging her into the room. Father Moule danced around us, ecstatically, and someone brought a chair for her, after which she let out a hollow and strange groan and sat down. Mr. Reane put away his worried expression and put on his usual composed and well-organized mask, but this time he couldn't hide his eagerness. His fingers trembled slightly, and his temples throbbed.
[1727] "I - left for a while," she croaked, licking her cracked lips, "and then I heard you were looking for me."
[1728] "Ah, you heard!" Father exclaimed, his face turning purple. "Where were you?"
[1729] "Hiding in a small cabin in the Adirondack Mountains," she answered wearily, "I wanted - to escape, you know? All this - this filthy, vulgar mess... was too much for me. To get away... away from civilization. No phone, no mail, nothing, not even a newspaper. But I had a radio..."
[1730] "That's Dr. Fosdick's cabin!" I exclaimed, suddenly remembering, "He must have been there that weekend his brother was murdered."
[1731] "Her heavy eyelids lifted and fell again, her cheeks sagging even more, looking like a sad old seal: "Yeah, that's right. It was his, I mean, the cabin was Eira's. It was... his love nest." She chuckled dryly, "He always took his girlfriends there. The week Joel died, he was with a prostitute..."
[1732] "Those don't matter now," Mr. Reen said calmly, "Mrs. Reen, what brought you back to Riddles?"
[1733] She shrugged: "It's funny, isn't it? I never knew I had it in me, and then I just broke down crying," she sat up straight, defiantly looking at him, "My conscience, that's what brought me back to Riddles!"
[1734] "Really, Miss Kaiser, I'm glad to hear you say that," he pulled a chair over and sat in front of her, we watched silently, "Mr. Allen Darrow was still in custody - just before the trial, wasn't he? - he sent you the last box, the one with the letter Z on top?"

[1742] "No."

[1745] She waved her plump arms: "No, not that. I'm not that worried. But first, I'd better tell you what that damn doll means and what Darrow has had on me, Joel, and Ella Fosdick for years."
[1746] It was a remarkable, incredible story. Years ago—twenty, twenty-five, she couldn't say how long—Joel and Ella Fossett were two American con artists traveling the world, making money any way they could, especially by fraud, because it was easy. They were using other names, it didn't matter. Fanny Kather was the daughter of an American dockside crook and thief exiled from Britain, running a small restaurant in Saigon, the capital of the French Indochina, at a time when it was an open and diverse city. The Fossett brothers came there, as she had said, looking for opportunities to "make money," and she met them. She "liked their style, they were two clever little con artists, brave and not too Christian."
[1747] The main customers of her restaurant were sailors, and she mixed with the scum and the decent seamen every day, listening to the secrets of the ships. Men, after all, who were not allowed to drink for weeks at sea, often let things slip once they were ashore. She got a valuable secret from the second mate of a docked cargo ship, who was drunk and leering at her. She flattered him into telling her the news. His ship was carrying an expensive but small cargo of uncut diamonds to Hong Kong.
[1748] "It was easy to arrange," she croaked, as I shivered to see the tired, fat old woman who had once been a pretty girl. "I told the Fossett brothers the secret, and we made a deal. Of course, they couldn't fool Fanny Kather, I didn't trust them, so I left the store unattended. So I went with them, posing as passengers on the ship.”
[1749] It was all so simple, the crew were Chinese and East Indian sailors, poor and foolish, easily frightened. The Fossett brothers stormed the armory, killed the sleeping captain, and the other senior officers, wounded or killed, and shot half the crew, stole the cargo, sank the ship, and escaped with Fanny Kather in a lifeboat. The Fossett brothers were sure that none of the crew survived, and they landed on a barren coast at night, divided the loot, and met again thousands of miles away after months.
[1750] "Who is Arlen Deaux?" Mr. Raine asked quickly.
[1751] She shuddered. "He was the second mate, the one who got drunk and told me the secret. I don't know how he managed to survive, but he did, and he didn't drown. I guess he swam to shore, and he's been holding a grudge against the Fuchs brothers and me ever since."
[1752] "Why didn't he report to a nearby port?" Father grumbled.

I mean, we were one and the same, and I couldn't let them out of my sight. We spent some time in New York City, and then we came up to New York State. [1754] We all fell silent, the tales of piracy, Vietnam, the sunken ship, the diamond heist, the murder of the crew, all the bloody stories seemed too unbelievable to be true, but they were all facts, coming from her mouth... and then I was called back to reality by Mr. Reen's calm voice.

[1767] Chapter XXI: The Final Clue
[1768] "Ah," Mr. Reen said calmly, and I realized – he had somehow understood it all in a way that was beyond me – a miracle had occurred. Then a serene smile spread across his face, the smile of a man who had worked hard and finally succeeded. He said nothing.
[1769] "He told me himself," Fanny Katherine said in a deep, agitated tone, the sobbing ceased, and she stared blankly at the wall, lost in memories. Ella and I had been watching Carmichael, and when Joel received the first small wooden box, he, Ella, and I – we talked about it, and that was the first time we knew Allen Darrow was still alive. We decided to keep it quiet, Joel – the senator!” She sniffed. "He was a coward, trying to bribe the informer, and Ella and I had to keep an eye on him."
[1770] This woman was lying, her eyes darting about, and I had no doubt that she would do anything, and I had no doubt that she had already decided on the night of Senator Fosdick's murder that if Allen Darrow proved difficult to control, she would kill him. I had no doubt that Senator Fosdick had the same plan in mind.
[1771] "The night Ella Fuscite was murdered," she croaked on, "it was just terrible, I went to his house again. [1773] Her eyes blazed: "I went to his house, and found Ella lying dead as a mackerel, on the floor of his office, with a knife in his chest."
[1774] The old gentleman suddenly looked concerned: "But you just said -"

[1776] "Damn it!" the father muttered. "I've seen this happen too many times before, just when they're about to say who killed them. Do you remember what he said –"

[1778] Father Muller stepped forward, his pale hand gripping her meaty one.

[1783] Chapter Twenty-Two: The Final Action
[1784] Mr. Reen gestured to me, "Percy, can you help me a big favor?" I rushed over to him, my breath becoming heavy, "Please call Governor Bruno for me, my disability—" He smiled and touched his ear, of course, he was completely deaf and could only communicate with the world through lip reading.

[1797] So we waited, for eight hours.
[1798] At nine o'clock, with two hours until the execution, a black limousine pulled up to Father Mulrooney's house, accompanied by four motorcycle state troopers. [1799] Father Mulrooney had been instructed by Mr. Lean to leave hours earlier, after being warned not to reveal any details of the plan.
[1800] Of course, he had to go to the death row, and from the conversation between the two old men before Father Mulrooney left, I guessed he would tell Allen Darrow to hold on to hope.
[1801] After washing, resting, and eating, Fanny Katherina sat quietly on the porch, a lonely old woman with red eyes and anxiety. We witnessed this historic gathering with mixed feelings. Governor Bruno's tense, straightforward, and restless posture was like that of a small horse, while Fanny Katherina appeared frightened and submissive, with Mr. Rene quietly watching.

[1804] "Oh, no," the old gentleman said mildly, "no, Bruno! This case must be dealt with using unexpected psychology to break through the killer's defenses, because you know, I don't have any actual evidence."
[1805] "So you know who killed the two brothers?" Governor Bruno asked slowly.

[1808] So we waited again.
[1809] With half an hour to go until eleven o'clock, we quietly left Father Mueller's house, leaving four tall young men in uniform behind, surrounding the weary and tired Fanny Katherine.
[1810] Our quiet group marched towards the gates of the Acoma prison, surrounded by darkness, with only the lights inside the prison shining like the eyes of a monster into the night sky.
[1811] The next thirty terrifying minutes I will never forget. I had no idea what Governor Rain and Mr. Rain had planned, but I was filled with fear that something would go wrong. However, as we walked through the archway and into the courtyard, everything seemed to go smoothly.
[1812] The governor himself put the guards on edge, his authority being undeniable, and we were immediately granted entry. We arrived at the courtyard outside the death chamber, where we could see the light from the death cell and feel the hurried preparations within the gray concrete walls. There was no sound in the four rooms, and the guards were all tense and uneasy in their movements.
[1813] The governor strictly ordered the guards to stay with us and not to tell any other prison staff about our presence. Everyone complied, although I caught a few curious glances... So we stood quietly in the dark corner of the well-lit courtyard, waiting.
[1814] The minute hand on my watch slowly crawled forward, and my father murmured to himself under his breath.
[1815] From Mr. Rain's tense expression, I understood that the most critical part of the plan was to wait until the last minute before the execution began. Of course, with the governor's presence, the danger to Deroda had been greatly reduced, but I still couldn't shake off my unease. As time passed, I grew increasingly intolerant, wanting to scream and protest, running madly through the courtyard and bursting into the imposing building in front of us...
[1816] With one minute left until eleven, the governor tensed up and shouted something to the guards before sprinting ahead of us, dashing through the courtyard, and arriving at the House of Death. We burst into the death row area of the death cell at exactly eleven o'clock. Fate was as cold as ice, as Bruno the governor brushed aside two guards, opened the door to the death chamber, and it was eleven minutes past the hour.
[1817] I will never forget the sight of the terrified faces inside the death chamber when we burst in. We seemed like a bunch of savages desecrating a modern Vesta temple or a group of Philistines trampling on a saint's altar. The scene – my memory is a series of fragmented images, each moment like a lifetime condensed into eternity. Every facial expression, every hand movement, or even a simple nod, remained frozen in time and space.
[1818] I was so overcome with emotion that I forgot that this sight was unprecedented in the history of executions. We had created the most dramatic moment in criminal history.
[1819] I looked at everyone and everything. Alan Deroulede, the poor wretch, sat on the electric chair with his eyes closed. One guard was tying his feet, another his torso, a third his arms, and a fourth paused in tying the blindfold over Alan's eyes. All four stopped their work, their mouths agape in astonishment. Magnum, the warden, stood a few feet away from the electric chair, holding a watch and standing motionless. Three other guards stood next to Father Mueller, who was on the verge of fainting and leaning on one of them. There were three officials from the court, and twelve witnesses. Among them, I noticed the pale face of Elijah Clay, whom Jeremy had mentioned earlier. There were two prison doctors, and the executioner stood in the small room, his left hand busy with some machinery...
[1820] The governor barked, "Warden, stop the execution!"
[1821] Allen Darrow opened his eyes, almost slightly surprised. The expression that disappeared from his face was like a signal, a spark of life reigniting the stage's motionless actors. The four guards surrounding the electric chair looked at each other in confusion, turning their heads to look at the warden. The warden blinked, staring blankly at his watch. Father Mueller silently murmured, his pale cheeks flushing with blood. Others opened their mouths and stared at each other, their voices buzzing, then suddenly silenced. Magnum, the warden, stepped forward: "But..."
[1822] Philo Vance quickly said, "Warden, Allen Darrow is innocent. We have obtained new evidence that can completely exonerate him of the murder charges for which he was convicted. Governor..."
[1823] Then something happened that, to the best of my knowledge, was unprecedented in any legal tragedy. Usually, when the execution chamber receives an order from the administrative authorities to suspend the execution, the condemned prisoner is immediately returned to his cell, and the witnesses and other attendees are immediately dispersed, and that's the end of it. But this time it was very special, and I now realize that the exposure must have taken place in the execution chamber. But what the governor and Mr. Rayne were trying to achieve through this melodramatic plot...
[1824] I think everyone was too scared to protest, and if any officials present questioned the appropriateness of this process, Governor Bruno's imposing jaw would have silenced them... When the old gentleman quietly walked over to the side of the electric chair, standing next to the little old man who had just been saved from death, everyone forgot everything. He spoke, and the whole room fell into a church-like silence.
[1825] In a concise, swift manner, and with a clarity of explanation that far exceeded my abilities, Philo Vance began by explaining the original reasoning of the murder of Senator Baskerville, stating that the left-handed Allen Darrow could not have been the perpetrator, and that the real killer was a right-handed person.
[1826] "Therefore," the old gentleman said with a voice filled with emotion and personal trembling, "we can reasonably assume that the killer who should have used his right hand deliberately used his left hand so that the investigators would think that Alan Dare was the killer. In other words, the killer wanted to use a crime that Alan Dare did not commit to 'frame' Alan Dare."
[1827] "Now, please pay attention," the old gentleman continued smoothly, "in order to frame Alan Dare, the killer must know what characteristics of Alan Dare? From the facts, there are three things: first, he must know that after serving his sentence in Apgun Prison, Alan Dare's right hand became paralyzed and now can only use his left hand. Second, he knows that on the night of the murder, Alan Dare indeed planned to visit Senator Baskerville; therefore, he also knows that Alan Dare will be formally released that day. Third, he knows that Alan Dare has a hypothetical motive to murder Senator Baskerville."
[1828] "Now let's discuss them one by one," the old gentleman continued smoothly, "who might know that Alan Dare's right hand became paralyzed in Apgun Prison? Warden Magnus told us that during the twelve years of his imprisonment, Alan Dare had never received any letters or visitors. Moreover, he had never sent any letters through regular channels. As for the illegal letter-carrying channel of the prison library assistant Taber, Alan Dare had only used it once: to send a blackmail letter to Senator Baskerville, and we know that the contents of the letter did not mention his arm. Furthermore, Alan Dare's right arm was paralyzed ten years ago, and until he was formally released, he had never left the prison walls. He had no family or friends. Indeed, there was one person from the outside who had visited Alan Dare during this period, Senator Baskerville himself, who had visited the prison's carpentry department - that opportunity allowed Alan Dare to recognize the senator. But based on the testimony, we also have reason to believe that the senator did not recognize Alan Dare, and since he did not notice Alan Dare in the presence of a crowd of prisoners, it is unlikely that he would remember that Alan Dare had a problem with his right arm. So we can rule out this possibility."
[1829] In the strong light, the death chamber was silent. So far, everything he had said I already knew, perhaps not as clearly, but I had already deduced the general direction. And I also understood that there would be what kind of conclusion. Everyone else stood still, their feet seemingly rooted to the floor beneath them.
[1830] "There is another possible explanation,"
[1831] "One of the two explanations is correct; which one is it? I will explain that the person who framed O' is directly related to Alcatraz, and this theory is more compelling and the correct answer."
[1832] "Following the assassination of Senator Bostwick, there were five sealed envelopes on his desk, one of which provided a significant clue, which I would not have known without Miss Percy Sampson, who reported the first murder to me with her remarkable photographic memory. The envelope had the impression of a backstitch needle - no, let me be more precise, not one, but 'two'. There were two clear impressions on the front of the envelope, one on the left and the other on the right. However, when the prosecutor opened the envelope, there was only 'one' backstitch pin inside! How could a backstitch needle leave two symmetrical impressions on the same front of an envelope?"
[1833] Someone took a deep breath, and the elderly gentleman leaned forward, obscuring Allen's Doe's still figure on the electric chair: "Let me tell you what happened. Senator Carlyle's secretary, Mr. McMichael, had seen his employer hurriedly insert the attachment into the envelope and then just as hurriedly seal it. Then, according to normal reasoning, Senator Carlyle would have left a backstamp mark on the envelope when he pressed it down, but we found two marks in different places. There can only be one explanation for this." He paused for a moment, "Someone opened the envelope, took out the attachment, and then, in the process of putting it back, accidentally flipped it over, making the attachment's position opposite to the original. Then, the envelope was sealed again, and when it was pressed down, the backstamp mark appeared on the other side of the envelope, because the position of the backstamp was completely different from the original."
[1834] "So, who will open that envelope?" the elderly gentleman continued, enunciating clearly, "As far as we know, there are only two possible candidates: one is the Senator himself; the other is the visitor who was seen entering and leaving the house alone during the time of the murder – as we have already established, this visitor is undoubtedly the perpetrator, and he also burned a letter, leaving behind the ashes we later found in the fireplace."
[1835] "Is it possible that the senator opened the envelope himself after Mr. Cummings left and before the visitor arrived? I admit, it's theoretically possible, but we must also follow general probability analysis. I ask you: why would he re-open his own letter? To correct errors? But there were no corrections made; all the contents of the letter were identical to the copy. To read through what he dictated and typed again? Nonsense! He had a copy right there on his desk to read."
[1836] "Putting that aside, if Senator Weston really wanted to open the envelope, he would have simply torn it open and replaced it with a new one, especially since he told Mr. Camichael that the letters would be mailed the next morning. However, the envelope has not been replaced, and there are two backstitch needle impressions on it. If it was a new envelope, there would only be one impression, so the envelope was not only opened but was the original one that was already sealed. How could that be? There was a warm electric coffee pot beside the desk, and it was still warm after the murder, so it is very clear (without any other evidence to explain how the envelope was opened) that the letter was softened with steam before it was opened. Ah, now we have reached the core of the problem!"
[1837] All the people in the room nodded repeatedly, obviously captivated by the old gentleman's reasoning, and he smiled and continued.
[1838] "If Senator Weston did not open the envelope, then it must have been opened by his visitor, who was the only person who entered and left the house during the murder."
[1839] "So, what was it on the envelope that attracted the visitor's attention - as I have already said, the visitor was the murderer - and drove him to violate all the principles of caution and open the envelope at the murder scene? The envelope was addressed to the warden of Acoma Prison and contained a 'case file on the promotion of Acoma'."
[1840] I glanced at Elijah Clay, whose face turned pale, and he was touching his chin with trembling fingers.
[1841] "Based on the two possibilities we have already deduced, you should remember: the first more likely hypothesis is that the murderer has a direct connection with the prison; the second less likely hypothesis is that the murderer has no connection with the prison. But he has a co-conspirator in the prison who can provide him with all the information he needs. Now, assuming the second possibility, that the murderer has no connection with the prison, but there is a snitch in the prison who informs him, what interest would prompt him to open a letter about the 'promotion proposal' for Aylesbury Prison? If he is not a person in the prison, he would not have any interest in it himself. Perhaps you will ask, what about the person who informs him in the prison? But why should the murderer care? If his co-conspirator's promotion does not affect the murderer himself; if his co-conspirator does not get promoted, the murderer is not harmed. So we can say that this hypothetical person outside the prison would not open the envelope at all.”
[1842] "Ah, but the murderer did open the envelope! Therefore, our more likely hypothesis must hold - generally speaking, a person who would be interested in the 'promotion proposal' for Aylesbury Prison and investigate its contents, I dare say, must have a direct connection with the prison.” He paused, his face shrouded in shadow, "In fact, when I tell you who the murderer is, you will find some reasons that are even more interesting than those I have just pointed out. Anyway, now, I should say, in general terms, that the murderer is a person in the prison.”
[1843] "From the facts of the first murder, we can deduce another inference, which I learned from Magnum Warden: the prison's routine schedule is very strict, for example, the guard's shifts are fixed and never changed. We have proven that the murderer is a person in Aylesbury Prison, when did he commit the murder of Senator Fairfax? At night. Therefore, whether he holds any position in the prison, he is obviously not a night-shift worker, otherwise he could not leave the prison to commit the murder at Senator Fairfax's house at night. So he is either a day-shift worker or a worker with no specific working hours. These are the basic elements, and when I deduce other developments, please remember these elements.”
[1844] As time passed, his voice grew sharper, and his face was etched with cold lines, and his gaze swept the room. I saw several witnesses sitting on hard benches, shivering slightly. The loud, echoing voice, the bright, dazzling light, the electric chair, and the motionless prisoner on it, the uniformed guards… I could not blame them for feeling uneasy, for I myself was shivering with my hair standing on end…
[1845] "Now," the old gentleman spoke clearly and briskly, "talking about the second
[1846] "So, what have we obtained? We have confirmed the previous conclusion - that the killer is someone from the prison - is correct. The intercepted note is a very strong circumstantial evidence that proves this was done by someone 'from the prison', who knows the secret communication system within the prison, intercepts Fuchs' note, and then replaces it with a forged note of his own."
[1847] "But, ladies and gentlemen, now we face the most crucial key to solving this case: why did the killer change the time of Deodato's escape from Tuesday to Wednesday? Because the killer planned to frame Deodato for the murder of Dr. Ella Fosdick, and since Deodato was innocent in the murder of Dr. Ella Fosdick, the real killer must - remember this - have had the time to kill Fosdick on the night Deodato escaped! If the killer changed the escape time from Tuesday to Wednesday, it means he couldn't kill Dr. Fosdick on Tuesday, but he could on Wednesday!" Jules Renet's thin face tightened, his fingers tapping, "Ha, you'll ask, why didn't he have time? From the first murder we learned, he wasn't a night worker, so 'any' night he had time to commit the crime, except Tuesday night. The only possible answer is, "he straightened up, pausing for a moment, "some unusual work in the prison, on Tuesday night, prevented the killer from being free!" But on the Tuesday night before Dr. Fosdick's murder, what unusual work was there in the prison that made someone who usually had free time on the night shift unavailable? I tell you, this case is about the heart and brain, and the conclusion is as eternal as natural law. On that Tuesday night, in this terrible death chamber, an execution by electrocution was held, and the name of the condemned was Scacchi. I will also tell you, the conclusion is as unbreakable as the Last Judgment: the killer of the Fosdick brothers must have been someone who attended Scacchi's electrocution!"
[1848] The room was as quiet as a vacuum, I dared not breathe, I dared not turn my head, I dared not move my eyes. No one dared to stir. Under the intense gaze of the elderly gentleman, we must have all looked like wax figures, standing by the electric chair, pouring out one word after another, detailing the plot of the crime and the inevitable tragedy.
[1849] "Let me enumerate," he finally spoke, his voice devoid of emotion, as cold as stalactites, "the necessary qualifications of this murderer—from the facts of the two murders, the limitations the murderer has drawn, are clear as if the murderer himself had engraved them on the bedrock of time."
[1850] "First, the killer is right-handed.
[1851] "Second, he is related to the Apgun Prison."
[1852] "Third, he is not a night duty officer."
[1853] "Fourth, he attended Scarcy's electrocution."
[1854] The room fell silent again, this time the silence seemed to vibrate like a tangible object that could be felt.
[1855] The old gentleman smiled and suddenly continued, "Everyone, I know you are shocked, especially because the people who attended Scarcy's electrocution and were related to Apgun Prison are here again in this special room! Because I learned from Director Magnum that the personnel who executed electrocution in Apgun Prison have never changed."
[1856] One of the guards let out a faint, hollow scream, and everyone turned to look at him before returning their gaze to Jules Renard.
[1857] "So," the old gentleman continued slowly, "let's eliminate the possible suspects one by one. Who attended Scarsdale's execution? Remember, the killer must meet the four qualifications I listed earlier. ... The 'twelve adult, respectable citizens' required by law for the death penalty, " he said to the stiff figures on the benches, "you have nothing to fear. According to the definition, you are all civilians and do not meet the second qualification. You must be eliminated from the list of possible suspects."
[1858] The person sitting in the second row of the bench sighed deeply, and several others carefully took out handkerchiefs to wipe the sweat from their foreheads.
[1859] "The three court officers required by law to oversee the execution can also be ruled out for the same reason."
[1860] The three of them shifted uneasily.
[1861] "The seven prison guards," Mr. Fogg continued in a dreamlike tone, "if I understood the warden correctly, I can assume that the same seven were present at Schussler's execution," he paused, "out! You are all night shift personnel - because you were on duty during the execution, which is fixed night duty - this clearly contradicts the third qualification. Therefore, you are not the murderer.
[1862] One guard muttered something in terror, and the tension in the room grew unbearable, with emotions on the verge of erupting. I glanced at my father, whose neck was turning red and seemed about to have a stroke. The governor stood still as a statue. Father Muller's eyes were blank. The warden was silent.
[1863] "The executioner," the calm and resolute voice continued to rise, "out! I saw him at Schussler's execution - fortunately, I attended that execution - he pressed the switch with his left hand twice, but according to the first qualification, the murderer is a right-hander."
[1864] I closed my eyes, and the pounding of my heart echoed in my ears. The old gentleman's voice fell silent, then suddenly resumed, filling the execution chamber with its echo.
[1865] "The two doctors required by law to attend the execution to confirm the death of the convict," he said to the two motionless doctors carrying black briefcases, "I couldn't exclude you earlier, but today Fanny Katherine provided a clue that allows me to eliminate you. Please allow me to explain."
[1866] "The murderer who pinned the blame for the murder of Dr. Fosdick on Dr. Deardorff knew that after he escaped, Dr. Deardorff would go to the doctor's office. So he had to be absolutely sure that the victim was dead, couldn't talk, and couldn't tell Dr. Deardorff or any unexpected visitor the name of the real murderer. In the case of Senator Fosdick's murder, the same principle applied. The killer stabbed him twice, the first time fatally, but he stabbed him again for good measure."
[1867] "We found three fingerprints of blood on Dr. Fosdick's wrist, of course, and the killer must have felt for the pulse. Why? Obviously, he wanted to make sure the victim was dead. But note this significant fact! His voice boomed like a thunderclap, "The killer checked the pulse of the victim, but he left the scene before the victim was dead. Fanny Kuster arrived later and found Dr. Fosdick still alive and able to prove Dr. Deardorff's innocence, although he didn't have time to name the real murderer before he died… You would ask, how does this rule out the two doctors who were present at the Scarcy electrocution and are here tonight? The answer is this."
[1868] "Let's assume that one of the doctors is the murderer, and the murder took place in 'the doctor's office.' A few feet away on the desk is the victim's medical kit—with various medical instruments, for example, a stethoscope. Yes, even a doctor, relying solely on a pulse, might fail to detect the most minute signs of life in a dying patient; but a doctor in a doctor's office, with all the necessary tools at hand, has to be sure the victim is dead. I would say, with the aid of a stethoscope, a doctor could easily determine whether the victim was dead or not…"
[1869] "Therefore, we can say that since a doctor has all the tools necessary to determine the victim's death, he would not leave the scene with the victim still alive. He would check the victim's pulse and stab him again to eliminate all signs of life. But the killer didn't do that, so the killer is not a doctor, and these two prison doctors can be eliminated."
[1870] I was so tense I wanted to scream, my father's knuckles were white with strain, and everyone else looked pale as death masks.
[1871] "As for Father Mulligan," Philo Vance's voice deepened, "the killer of the Fulton brothers is the same person, but Dr. Fulton was killed around 11 o'clock. From 10 o'clock, Father Mulligan was with me on his porch, and it was impossible for him to carry out this murder. Therefore, he could not have killed Senator Fulton."
[1872] The pale faces in front of me were covered with a flickering red shadow, and I heard a voice that shook my soul say, "There are twenty-seven people in this room, and only one of them is the killer of the Fulton brothers. We have eliminated twenty-six, and now there is only one left, he ... grab him, don't let him escape! Sam, don't let him shoot!"
[1873] The room erupted in screams, shouts, screams, and struggles. The person at the center of the storm was now being held tightly by my father's iron hands. The face twisted into a purple hue, with eyes burning with madness, was that of Warden Magnus.
[1874] Chapter 23: Last Words
[1875] Looking back at the content of this book, I wonder if I will reveal that the killer of Senator Fulton and Dr. Fulton is someone other than Warden Magnus. Although it is difficult to determine, I think it is unlikely.
[1876] For me, in many places, the astounding truth is almost self-evident. I have studied many techniques of writing detective stories, whether based on fact or fiction, and perhaps in this book, you can find Philo Vance – and subtly my own views – deducing the answer from every point, in different ways. As we pursue the killer, this is just a process of cross-checking; and even more so, while reading, this is a process of cross-checking the solution. … To allow the reader to make the most appropriate judgment, I have made every effort to re-present the entire process of solving the case. I cannot adopt the analysis framework of the old gentleman, and we know nothing about his meticulous thinking. We simply do not have his insight and sensitivity to apply it.
[1877] I know, there are still some things that haven't been explained yet, and for the sake of the story's completeness, they must be addressed. Although we later found out that these things were not important to the case, such as the motive of Magus Warden for committing the crime – some people would say that he was the least likely to be tempted to commit such a bloodbath. However, someone told me that according to the records, there is a former warden who is now serving a sentence in the prison, and given his experience in enforcing the law, it is hard to believe that he would commit such a crime.
[1878] Magnus later confessed in his confession that he was motivated by money. After a long and blameless life, he seemed to have some savings, but lost them all in the stock market. He looked back on his life and found himself penniless, and then Senator Bessett came knocking, showing an unusual interest in Deo and even mentioning blackmail. On the day Deo was officially released from prison, just as Magnus had said, Senator Bessett called him, saying that he had decided to pay Deo the $50,000. Poor Magnus! He was in dire need of money, and temptation overcame everything. That evening, he went to Senator Bessett's house, not entirely thinking about it, not knowing that Deo held something over the Bessett brothers.
[1879] However, when faced with the senator, perhaps seeing the money, he blindly and quickly made up his mind to commit murder. He planned to kill the senator, steal the money, and frame Deo. So he picked up the paper knife on the desk and committed an incredible crime. Then, when he checked the scene, he found that the topmost letter in the stack of letters was a letter to his brother, Dr. Fuchs, which gave him an idea. So Dr. Fuchs was also involved! The letter mentioned the ship, the Hijaz Star, and this served as the starting point for him to easily uncover the relationship between Deo and the Fuchs brothers and the entire truth of the story. He burned the letter to prevent it from falling into the hands of the police. If the truth came out, he could not blackmail Dr. Fuchs, but if only he and Deo knew about it, Deo would be sentenced to death for murdering the senator, and Magnus could then blackmail Dr. Fuchs at will.
[1880] This plan seems flawless, but Allen Darrow is not executed for the murder of Senator Fulton, but instead receives a life sentence. From Magnus' perspective, this seems like a good opportunity to use the man again. He waits, and in a chance encounter, he learns that the cunning Taber runs an underground network, smuggling documents in and out of the prison undetected. Magnus remains calm, waiting for the opportunity to arise. The chance comes when he examines the documents hidden in Father Mueller's prayer book, intercepting a note from Dr. Fulton to Darrow one day, reading it behind Taber's back, and learning of Darrow's escape plan and discovering a golden opportunity. However, the escape plan is set for Tuesday, while on Tuesday Magnus must oversee Scarcy's execution, so he forges a note from Darrow, changing the escape date to Wednesday - a day when Magnus will be free. On the back of the note he intercepted from Dr. Fulton, he forges Darrow's handwriting in bold print, secretly giving the note to Dr. Fulton to explain that Darrow will not escape on Tuesday. As with all criminal events, he becomes increasingly entangled, willing to do anything to achieve his goal. When he sends out that note, it appears safe on the surface, but it ultimately exposes him.
[1881] There are a few more small matters. I recall the following day we were sitting on Father Mowell's veranda, and Elijah Clary asked why the Warden of Magus had torn open the letter addressed to him from Senator Fosdick's desk, marked "Agnus promotion case."
[1882] The elderly gentleman sighed, "This question is quite interesting. Do you remember, in my analysis last night, I proposed an inciting explanation? I think I know why Magnus opened that letter. In my original analysis, anyone in the prison could have opened the letter, anyone could have, except the warden, because the letter was addressed to him, and 'The Agnew Promotion Case' would not affect his position. Therefore, when my later analysis inevitably pointed to Magnus, I asked myself, why did he open that letter? Because he thought the content of the letter was different from the hint on the envelope! The Senator had hinted to Magnus during his visit to the prison that Deere had something on him, and Magnus thought that the content of the letter might refer to that visit, and if the letter fell into the hands of the police, it would implicate Magnus. Of course, this self-assumed cautious idea was wrong, as he was in an unusually emotional state and could not think clearly. In any case, the general rule could not explain the real reason."
[1883] "So," the father asked, "who sent the second segment of the small box to Ella Forte and the third segment to Fanny Kather? It's impossible for D'Oyly to do it. This part confuses me."
[1884] "I can't figure it out either," I muttered.
[1885] "I think I know who the anonymous helper is," Philo Vance smiled, "it's our friend Mark Corry, the lawyer. I'm not entirely sure, but while Deaver was waiting for his verdict, he must have asked Corry to send out the remaining two crates at some point. I suppose Deaver had the boxes and the letter hidden in a regular parcel for mailing. I don't think Corry is too bound by social norms, so he might have discovered the blackmail and decided to make a little money from it. But don't say it was me who said it."
[1886] "Until we prove Derouche innocent," Father Mulvey asked timidly, "isn't it dangerous to put Derouche on the edge of death?"
[1887] The old gentleman's smile faded: "It's necessary. Father, please remember, I have no evidence that can bring Magnus to justice in court. We must make him enter an abnormal state of emotional excitement, I arranged the timing of the analysis, precisely grasped the tense atmosphere on the scene, and witnessed the result. When he faced an accusation with no evidence - as I predicted, he was stupid and blindly - he tried to escape! Poor guy. " He paused for a moment, "He confessed later. If we had taken a conventional approach, Magnus would have had time to protect himself, think things through, and cleverly deny all the accusations. Without evidence, it would be difficult to convict him, even if it were possible."
[1888] Later, John Hume was elected as the state senator of Tyrell County. Elyot Clay's marble company business declined slightly, but at least it was cleaner than before. Fanny Kather was sentenced to serve a long term in federal prison...
[1889] I haven't mentioned what happened to Allen Derouche, the protagonist of all this commotion, and also the victim of a mad killer's conspiracy. It seems intentional, I guess, that I have been reluctant to mention the poor Derouche. I think - oh, it's his last fate. Destiny has determined that, whether he is innocent in these murder cases or not, he is a useless person to society.
[1890] Anyway, when Mr. Vance announced his conclusion and Magnus, the warden, was captured, the old gentleman turned happily to the poor wretch sitting on the electric chair. However, when he tried to take Derouche away from the nightmare seat of the legal punishment, we saw him still sitting very quietly, even with a smile on his face.
[1891] Derwood died, and the doctor said it was from heart failure. I was frightened for several weeks, wondering if he had died from excitement. I'll never know. Although he was listed on the case sheet of Apgan Prison as having a very weak heart since twelve years ago when he was first incarcerated.
[1892] There's one more thing.
[1893] The day after Mr. Reen supplemented our explanation, young Jeremy took my arm and led me out for a walk. I must say, he planned it very well. The events of the night before had left me a little mentally exhausted, and in that situation, I was extremely lacking in self-control.
[1894] Nevertheless, Jeremy carefully held my hand. Then, I simplified this long story as best I could, and he asked me in a confused tenor if I would like to become Mrs. Jeremy Clay.
[1895] What a good boy! I looked at his curls and broad shoulders and thought, it's so sweet and satisfying to know that someone cares about you and wants to marry you. His tall and healthy young figure, despite his praise of the merits of vegetarianism, didn't matter, because even the clever Shaw could do so - although occasionally I like to taste the flavor of smoked steak myself...
[1896] I pondered the excuses. It was not that I did not love Jeremy, and from the standpoint of the novel, to have the hero and heroine, at the end of the story, embracing each other in the sunset view, and saying, "Oh, dear Jeremy--I do, I do!" -- that could not have been more beautiful.
[1897] And yet I took his hand in mine, and stood on tiptoe to kiss the indentation in the centre of his chin, and said, "Oh, dear Jeremy-no."
[1898] I said it very sweetly; he was so good that one could not bear to hurt him. Yet Patience Sam was not fit for marriage, and I was a forward-looking young girl, and I could vaguely foresee that in a few years' time I would be standing at the right hand of that marvellous old man in a well-dressed shirt and big shoes, and he would direct my path-Hallelujah-! -I'll be his female partner, and we'll join forces to answer everyone's criminal mysteries. ...... So silly, isn't it?
[1899] And, say really, if it weren't for father-he's a dear old dad, but just a little less cerebral-I'd change my name to something uniquely unassuming, like Zeria Raine or something. That name sounds so much more cerebral.
